The question of conflict of Parliamentary supremacy and executive overbearing is more imaginary than actual or real.	None
The reinduction of the Government of the State also besets with several in congruities.	None
It cannot be assumed that the President lightly removed the State Government.	None
It must be for formidable grounds, though not judicially discoverable nor discernible to strict judicial scrutiny.	None
All the proclamations so far issued were not disapproved by the Parliament.	None
The dismissed Government, if restituted into power, may violate with impunity the provisions of the Constitution and laws for the balance period taking advantage of majority in the Legislature and full scale corruption or other unconstitutional acts will have their free play.	None
The political party itself and all their members of the Legislature should collectively own responsibility for the removal of their Government and their unconstitutional governance write its own death warrant.	None
Restitution thereby puts a premium on failing the Constitution.	None
The political party must seek a fresh mandate from the electorates and establish their credibility by winning majority seats.	None
The existence of the Legislative Council which is not dissolvable like Rajya Sabha, cannot by itself transact any business, in particular the finance bills or appropriate bills or annual financial statements.	None
Therefore, its continuance shall render no. criteria to the continuance of Legislature or to assume it be not dissolved on Grammanian rule to reconstitute the dissolved Legislative Assembly of which the majority members belong to the same party.	None
No. doubt dissolution of the Legislature literally would include Legislative Council but not every State has a Council.	None
No. distinction between two types of States, one with Council and another without Council and the former would be eligible for revival and latter perforce would not be, was not meant by the Constitution.	None
Grammanian rules carries no. consistence.	None
Moreover this problem could also be tested from the expediency and functional efficacy.	None
The possibility of reinduction creates functional hiatus.	None
Suppose the Court grants stay till the Parliament approves the proclamation, if urgent need arose to issue ordinance or.	None
transact legislative or financial business, who would do it ?	None
The suspended Assembly cannot do nor the Parliament.	None
The dismissed Ministry cannot transact the legislative business.	None
Even if permitted to function and ultimately the proclamation is approved by the Parliament, what would happen to the validity of the. executive and legislative acts done in the interregnum.	None
As stated, is there no. possibility of large scale abuse of office for personal or political gain ?	None
If the orders are issued by the Courts on valuebased opinion, where is the finality and at what point a stop is to be put ?	None
If stay is granted, by a High Court and writ petition is not disposed of and the term of the Legislative Assembly expires what would happen to the Ministry in office?	None
Whether it would continue by order of the Court?	None
How elections are to be conducted by the Election Commission?	None
Is it under the orders of the Court or by the exercise of the power under Article 324 ?	None
Is day to day executive, legislative and administrative actions are to be done under the writ of the Court?	None
If a High Court issues a direction to allow the dissolved Assembly its full course of balance period including the suspended period what would happen?	None
Is it not violative of Article 172 ?	None
Whether it could be prevented to be done ?	None
If such Order is not complied with, is not the President liable to contempt of the Court and if so what happens to the protection of Article 361 ?	None
Instead of solving the problems, does not the writ of the Court creates constitutional crisis ?	None
Giving deep and anxious consideration and visualising the farreaching constitutional crisis, we are firmly of the view that the selfrestraint constrains us to express no. value opinion, leaving it to the Parliament to ponder over and if deemed necessary amend Art.	None
356 suitably.	None
The Constitution was amended more than 77 times and Article 356 itself was amended six times through the Constitutions 38th Amendment Act 42nd Amendment Act 44th Amendment Act 59th Amendment Act 64th Amendment Act and 68th Amendment Act.	None
Apart from the Congress Party, three nonCongress political parties were in power at the Centre during these 44 years and no. amendment was brought to Article 356(3) that on disapproval of the Proclamation by the Parliament the dissolved Assembly stands revived and removed Government stood reinducted.	None
The statutory construction fortifies this conclusion.	None
CASUS OMISSUS  WHETHER PERMISSIBLE TO SUPPLY The question, further arises whether by interpretative process, would it be permissible to fill in the gaps.	None
Though it is settled law that in working the law and finding yawning gaps therein, to give life and force to the legislative intent, instead of blaming the draftsman, the Courts ironed out the creases by appropriate technique of interpretation and infused life into dry bones of law.	None
But such an interpretation in our respectful view is not permissible, when we are called upon to interpret the organic Constitution and working the political institutions created therein.	None
When Parliament has had an opportunity to consider what exactly is going wrong with the political system designed by the Constitution but took no. steps to amend the Constitution in this behalf, it is a principle of legal policy, that the law should be altered deliberately, rather than casually by a sidewind only, by major and considered process.	None
Amendment of the Constitution is a serious legislative business and change in the basic law, carefully workout, more fundamental changes are brought out by more thorough going and indepth consideration and specific provisions should be made by which it is implemented.	None
Such is the way to contradict the problem by the legislative process of a civilised State.	None
It is a well established principle of construction that a statute is not to be taken as affecting Parliamentary alteration in the general law unless it shows words that are found unmistakably to that conclusion.	None
No. motive or bad faith is attributable to the Legislature.	None
Bennion at page 338 extracting from the Institute of the Law of Scotland, Vol. 3, page 1 of The Practice by David Maxwell at page 127 abstracted that Where a matter depends entirely on the construction of the words of a statute, there cannot be any appeal to the nobile officium.	None
He stated at page 344 that where the literal meaning of the enactment goes narrower than the object of the legislator, the Court may be required to apply a rectifying construction.	None
Now a days it is regarded as not in accordance with public policy to allow a craftsmans ineptitude to prevent justice being done.	None
This was not always the case.	None
Where the language of a statute is clear and unambiguous, there is no. room for the application either of the doctrine of casus omissus or of pressing into service external aid, for in such a case the words used by the Constitution or the statute speak for themselves and it is not the function of the court to add words or expressions merely to suit what the courts think is the supposed intention of the Legislature.	None
In American Jurisprudence, 2 Series, Vol. 73 at page 397 in para 203 it is stated that It is a general rule that the courts may not, by construction insert words or phrases in a statute or supply a casus omissus by giving force and effect to the language of the statute when applied to a subject about which nothing whatever is said, and which, to all appearances, was not in the minds of the Legislature at the time of the enactment of the law.	None
Under such circumstances new provisions or ideas may not be interpolated in a statute or ingrafted thereon.	None
At page 434 in para 366 it is further stated that While it has been held that it is duty of the courts to interpret a statute as they find it without reference to whether its provisions are expedient or unexpedient.	None
It has also been recognised that where a statute is ambiguous and subject to more than one interpretation, the expediency of one construction or the other is properly considered.	None
Indeed, where the arguments are nicely balanced, expediency may tip the scales in favour of a particular construction.	None
It is not the function of a court in the interpretation of statues, to vindicate the wisdom of the law.	None
The mere fact that the statute leads to unwise results is not sufficient to Justify the court in rejecting the plain meaning of unambiguous words or in giving to a statute a meaning of which its language is not susceptible, or in restricting the scope of a statute.	None
By the same token an omission or failure to provide for contingencies which it may seem wise to have provided for specifically, does not justify any judicial addition to the language of the statute.	None
To the contrary, it is the duty of the Courts to interpret a statute as they find it without reference to whether its provisions are wise or unwise, necessary or unnecessary appropriate or inappropriate, or well or illconceived.	None
Craies on Statute Law, 7th Edition, at page 69 states that the second consequence of the rule of casus omissus is that the statute may not be extended to meet a case for which provision has clearly and undoubtedly not been made.	None
In Construction of Statutes by Crawford at page 269 in paragraph 169 it is stated that omissions in a statute cannot, as a general rule, be supplied by construction.	None
Thus, if a particular case is omitted from the terms of a statute, even though such a case is within the obvious purpose of the statute and the omission appears to have been due to accident or inadvertence, the court cannot include the omitted case by supplying the omission.	None
This is equally true where the omission was due to the failure of the Legislature to foresee the missing case.	None
As is obvious, to permit the court to supply the omissions in statutes, would generally constitute an encroachment upon the field of the Legislature.	None
In construing the Constitution we cannot look beyond the letter of the Constitution to adopt something which would command itself to our minds as being implied from the context.	None
In State of Tasmania v. The Commonwealth of Australia and State of Victoria, 1904 (1) CLR 329 , 35859, Connor, J. dealing with the question observed thus It appears to me the only safe rule is to look at the statute itself and to gather from it what is its intention.	None
If we depart from that rule we are apt to run the risk of the danger described by Pollack, C.B., in Mille v. Solomons.	None
If, he says, the meaning of the language be plain and clear, we have nothing to do but to obey it  to administer it as we find it and, I think, to take a different course is to abandon the office of Judge, and to assume the province of legislation.	None
Some passages were cited by Mr. Glynn from Black on the Interpretation of Laws, which seem to imply that there might be a difference in the rules of interpretation to be applied to the Constitution and those to be applied to any other Act of Parliament, but there is no. foundation for any such distinction.	None
The intention of the enactment is to be gathered from its words.	None
If the words are plain, effect must be given to them if they are doubtful, the intention of Legislature is to be gathered from the other provisions of the statute aided by a consideration of surrounding circumstances.	None
In all cases in order to discover the intention you may have recourse to contemporaneous circumstances  to the history of the law, and you may gather from the instrument itself the object of the Legislature in passing it.	None
In considering the history of the law, you may look into previous legislation, you must have regard to the historical facts surrounding the bringing of law into existence.	None
In the case of a Federal Constitution the field of inquiry is naturally more extended than in the case of a State Statute, but the principles to be applied are the same.	None
You may deduce the intention of the Legislature from a consideration of the instrument itself in the light of these facts and circumstances, but you cannot go beyond it.	None
If that limitation is to be applied in the interpretation of an ordinary Act of Parliament, it should at least be as stringently applied in the interpretation of an instrument of this kind, which not only is a statutory enactment, but also embodies the compact by which the people of the several colonies of Australia agreed to enter into an indissoluble Union.	None
In Encyclopedia of the American Judicial System the constitutional interpre tation by Craig R. Ducat it is stated that the standard for assessing constitutionality must be the words of the Constitution, not what the Judges would prefer the Constitution to mean.	None
The constitutional supremacy necessarily assume that a superior rule is what the Constitution says, it is not what the Judges would prefer the Constitution to mean.	None
The constitution supremacy necessarily assumes that a superior rule is what the Judges prefer it to be.	None
(Vide page 973) (emphasis supplied).	None
In Judicial tributes balancing the competing interest Prof. Ducat quoted with approval the statement of Bickel at page 798 thus The judicial process is too principleprone and principlebound  it has to be, there is no. other justification or explanation for the role it plays.	None
It is also too remote from conditions, and deals, case by case, with too narrow a slice of reality.	None
It is not accessible to all the varied interests that are in play in any decision of great consequence.	None
It is, very properly, independent.	None
It is passive.	None
It has difficulty controlling the stages by which it approaches a problem.	None
It rushes forward too fast, or it lags its pace hardly ever seems just right.	None
For all these reasons, it is, in a vast, complex, changeable society, a most unsuitable instrument for the formation of policy.	None
In the modes of Constitutional Interpretation by Craig R. Ducat, 1978 Edition at p. 125, he stated that the Judges decision ought to mean societys values not their own.	None
He quoted Cardozos passage from the Nature of Judicial Process at page 108 that, a Judge, I think would err if he were to impose upon the community as a rule of life his own idiosyncracies of conduct or belief The court when caught in a paralysis of dilemma should adopt selfrestraint, it must use the judicial review with greatest caution.	None
In clash of political forces in political statement the interpretation should only be in rare and suspicious occasions to nullify ultra vires orders in highly arbitrary or wholly irrelevant proclamation which does not bear any nexus to the Predominant purpose for which the Proclamation was issued, to declare it to be unconstitutional and no.	None
more.	None
Frankfurter, J. says in Dennis v. United States, 1950 (341) US 474 , 525, thus But how are competing interests to be assessed ?	None
Since they are not subject to quantitative ascertainment, the issue necessarily resolves itself into asking, who is to make the adjustment ?  who is to balance the relevant factors and ascertain which interest is in the circumstances to prevail ?	None
Full responsibility for the choice cannot be given to the courts.	None
Courts Are not representative bodies.	None
They are not designed to be a good reflex of a democratic society.	None
Their judgment is best informed, and therefore most dependable, within narrow limits.	None
Their essential quality is detachment, founded on independence.	None
History teaches that the independence of the Judiciary is jeopardized when courts become embroiled in the passions of the day and assume primary responsibility in choosing between competing political, economic and social pressures Regionalism, lingualism and religious fundamentalism have become divisive forces to weaken the unity and intergrity of the country.	None
Lingualistic chauvinism aiding its fuel to keep the people poles apart.	None
Communalism and casteism for narrow political gains are creating foul atmosphere.	None
The cessassionist forces are working from within and out side the country threatening national integration to preserve the unity and integrity of the nation, it is necessary to sustain the power of the President to wisely use Article 356 to stop them out and keep the Government of the State function in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Article 356 should, therefore, be used sparingly in only cases in which the exercise of the power is called for.	None
It is not possible to limit the scope of action under Article 356 to, specific situations, since the failure of the constitutional machinery may occur in several ways due to diverse causes be it political, internal subversion or economic causes and no.	None
straight jacket formula would be possible to evolve, The founding fathers thus confided the exercise of the power in the highest executive, the President of India, through his Council of Ministers aided by the Prime Minister of the country who is accountable to the people of country.	None
STAY OF ELECTIONS WHETHER COULD BE MADE Under Art.	None
168 for every State there shall be Legislative Assembly and in some States Legislative Council.	None
Article 172(l) provides that every Legislative Assembly of every State, unless sooner dissolved shall continue for five years from the date appointed for its first meeting and no longer and the expiration of such period of five years shall operate as a dissolution of the Assembly.	None
The proviso to Clause (1) or subclause (2) are not relevant.	None
It is thereby declared the constitutional policy that five years tenure of the Legislature starts running from the date appointed for its first meeting and expiration of the period operates constitutionally as date of dissolution of the Assembly.	None
The phrase no longer reinforces its mandatory character.	None
Article 324(l) enjoins the Election Commission to conduct elections to the Parliament and to the Legislature of every State etc.	None
The R.P. Act, Rules and the instructions prescribes the procedure to conduct and complete elections four months before the expiry of the date of dissolution.	None
Article 329(b) issues an injunction that no election to either House of Parliament or to the House of the Legislature of a State shall be called in question except by an election petition presented to such authority and in such manner as may be provided for by or under any law made by the appropriate Legislature.	None
In other words, the election process once get in motion should run its full course and all election disputes shall be resolved in accordance with the procedure established by R.P. Act.	None
In N. P. Punnuswami v. Returning Officer, Namakkal Constituency, at the earliest Constitution Bench of this Court held that having regard to the important functions which the Legislatures have to perform in democratic countries, it has always been recognised to be a matter of first importance that elections shall be concluded as early as possible according to the time schedule and all controversial matters and all disputes arising out of elections should be postponed till after the elections are over, so that the election proceedings may not be unduly retarded or protracted.	None
In Lakshmi Charan Sen v. A.K.M. Hassan Uzzaman, another Constitution Bench considered the effect of interim stay of general elections to West Bengal Legislative Assembly granted by the Calcutta High Court in a writ proceedings held that the High Court must observe selfimposed limitation on their power to act under Article 226 by refusing to pass orders or giving directions which will inevitably result in an indefinite postponement of elections to legislative bodies, which are the very essence of the democratic foundation and functioning of our Constitution.	None
That limitation ought to be observed irrespective of the fact whether the preparation and publication of electoral rolls are a part of the process of election within the meaning of Article 329(b) of the Constitution.	None
It is the duty of the Court to protect and preserve the integrity of the Constitutional institutions which are devised to foster democracy and when the method of their functioning is questioned, which is open to the citizen to do, the court must examine the allegations with more than ordinary care.	None
Very often the exercise of jurisdiction especially the writ jurisdiction involves questions of propriety rather than of power.	None
The fact that the Court has power to do a certain thing does not mean that it must exercise that power regardless of consequences.	None
Holding the elections to the Legislatures and holding them according to law are both matters of paramount importance and is the constitutional obligation imposed by Article 168.	None
The pragmatic approach was Couched at 523 (of SCR) (at p. 1246 of AIR) thus India is an oasis of democracy a fact of contemporary history which demands of the Courts the use of wise statesmanship in the exercise of their extraordinary powers under the Constitution.	None
The High Courts must observe a selfimposed limitation on their power to act under Art. 226, by refusing to pass order or give directions which will inevitably result in an indefinite postponement of elections to legislative bodies, which are the very essence of the democratic foundation and functioning of our Constitution.	None
That limitation ought to be observed irrespective of the fact whether the preparation and publication of electoral rolls are a part of the process of election within the meaning of Article 329(b) of the Constitution There are plethora of precedents in this behalf.	None
But suffice for the limited purpose to say that the exercise of the power either under Article 226 or Article 32 or Article 136 staying the elections to the dissolved Assembly under Article 356 not only flies in the face of the constitutional mandates and the law laid down by this Court, but creates uncertainty and constitutional crisis as stated hereinbefore.	None
Enlightened public opinion both inside or outside the Parliament, informed public objective criticism, objective assessment of the ground realities would inhibit misuse of power and hinder highly irrational exercise of the power.	None
The question, finally emerges is whether issuance of the Proclamation under Article 356 without affording a particular Chief Minister to test his majority support of his party in the Legislatures of Janta Dal or coalition on the floor of the House is arbitrary and bears no.	Issue
reasonable nexus or irrational.	Issue
Having given our anxious consideration to the facts in Bommais case and in the light of the discussion made hereinbefore that the fluid situation prevailing during the relevant period appears to have persuaded the President that he had constitutional duty to maintain the purity of the democratic process and required to stamp out horsetrading among the Legislatures which had resulted in the failure of the constitutional machinery, satisfied himself that necessitated to issuance of the Proclamation under Article 356.	None
Though the majority strength of the ruling party or coalition in the Legislative Assembly may be tested on the floor of the House and may be a salutory principle as recommended by the Conference of the Governors, it would appear that in its working there emerged several pitfalls and so it was not found enforceable as a convention.	None
It is for the political parties or the Chief Ministers conference to take a decision in that behalf and it is not judicially manageable for the Court to give any declaration in this behalf.	None
In regard to dissolution of U.P. Assembly, though there is no. writ petition filed, since the Government machinery of that Government had failed to prevent destruction of Sri Ram JanambhoomiBabri Masjid disputed structure and failed to protect the religious property, be it belong to Hindus or Muslims and in that surged atmosphere when it was done, it cannot be concluded that the President acted unconstitutionally or that there is no. proximate nexus between the action and the demolition to exercise the power under Article 356.	None
Equally regarding dissolution of Legislative Assemblies of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh, the reports of the Governors do disclose that some of the Ministers and some Chief Ministers actively associated or encouraged Kar Sewaks to participate in the demolition of Ram JanambhoomiBabri Masjid disputed structure and also criticised the imposition of ban on R. S. S	None
The law and order situation or public order situation do not appear to have been brought under control.	None
The common thread of breach of secularism ran through the events andwith prognosis action was taken.	None
Our learned brother Jeevan Reddy, J. elaborately considered the pleadings of the parties and arguments by the respective counsel.	None
He also deduced the conclusions.	None
The need for discussion once over is thereby redundant.	None
We respectfully agree with him and in case of Meghalaya also.	None
We conclude that the satisfaction reached by the President cannot be adjudicated with any judicially discoverable and manageable standards, but one stark fact that emerged is that due to sustained compaign by the BJP and other organisations Sri Ram JanambhoomiBabri Masjid disputed structure was destroyed.	None
Consequential situation that has arisen due to which the President satisfied that Governments of the States of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution and they breached the basic features of the Constitution, namely secularism.	None
Therefore the satisfaction reached by the President cannot be said to be irrelevant warranting interference.	None
As regards Meghalaya is concerned, though a declaration may possibly be made on the validity of the Presidential Proclamation, since the elections have already been held, its need became fiet accompli.	None
CONCLUSIONS Federalism envisaged in the Constitution of India is a basic feature in which the Union of India is permanent within the territorial limits set in Article 1 of the Constitution and is indestructible.	None
The State is the creature of the Constitution and the law made by Articles 2 to 4 with no. territorial integrity, but a permanent entity with its boundaries alterable by a law made by the Parliament.	None
Neither the relative importance of the legislative entries in Schedule VII, Lists I and II of the Constitution, nor the fiscal control by the Union per se are decisive to conclude that the Constitution is unitary.	None
The respective legislative powers are traceable to Articles 245 to 254 of the Constitution.	None
The State qua the Constitution is federal in structure and independent in its exercise of legislative and executive power.	None
However, being the creature of the Constitution the State has no.	None
right to secede or claim sovereignty.	None
Qua the Union, State is quasifederal.	None
Both are coordinating institutions and ought to exercise their respective powers with adjustment, understanding and accommodation to render socioeconomic and political justice to the people, to preserve and elongate the constitutional goals including secularism.	None
The preamble of the Constitution is an integral part of the Constitution.	None
Democratic form of Government, federal structure, unity and integrity of the nation, secularism, socialism, social justice and judicial review are basic features of the Constitution.	None
The office of the Governor is a vital link and a channel of impartial and objective communication of the working of the Constitution by the State Government to the President of India.	None
He is to ensure protection and sustenance of the constitutional process of the working of the Constitution in the State playing an impartial role.	None
As head of the executive he should truthfully with high degree of constitutional responsibility inform the President that a situation has arisen in which the constitutional machinery has failed and the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution with necessary factual details in a non partisan attitude.	None
The Union of India shall protect the State Government and as corollary under Article 356 it is enjoined that the Government of every State should be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
On receipt of a report from the Governor or otherwise the President (Council of Ministers) on being satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the Government of a State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution, is empowered to issue proclamation under Article 356(1) and impose President Rule in the State in the manner laid down in clauses (a) to (c), of Art. 356(l) of the Constitution.	None
The exercise of the power under Article 356 is an extraordinary one and need to be used sparingly when the situation contemplated by Art.	None
356 warrants to maintain democratic form of Government and to prevent paralysing of the political process.	None
Single or individual act or acts of violation of the Constitution for good, bad or indifferent administration does not necessarily constitute failure of the constitutional machinery or characterises that a situation has arisen in which the Government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
The exercise of power under Art.	Arguments of Petitioner
356 should under no.	None
circumstances be for a political gain to the party in power in the Union Govt.	None
It should be used sparingly and with circumspection that the Govt.	None
of the State function with responsibility in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Rule of law has been chosen as an instrument of social adjustment and resolution of conflicting social problems to integrate diverse sections of the society professing multireligious faiths, creed, caste or region fostering among them fraternity, transcending social, religious, linguistic or regional barriers.	None
Citizenship is either by birth or by domicile and not as a member of religion, caste, sect, region or language.	None
Secularism has both positive and negative contents.	None
The Constitution struck a balance between temporal parts confining it to the person professing a particular religious faith or belief and allows him to practise, profess and propagate his religion, subject to public order, morality and health.	None
The positive part of secularism has been entrusted to the State to regulate by law or by an executive order The State is prohibited to patronise any particular religion as State religion and is enjoined to observe neutrality.	None
The State strikes a balance to ensue an atmosphere of full faith and confidence among its people to realise full growth of personality and to make him a rational being on secular lines, to improve individual excellence, regional growth, progress and national integrity.	None
Religion being susceptible to the individuals or groups of people professing a particular religion antagonistic to another religion or groups of persons professing different religion, brings inevitable social or religious frictions.	None
if religion is allowed to overplay, social disunity is bound to erupt leading to national disintegration.	None
Secularism is a part of the basic features of the Constitution.	Reasoning
Political parties, group of persons or individual who would seek to influence electoral process with a view to come to political power, should abide by the Constitution and the laws including secularism, sovereignty, integrity of the nation.	None
They he should not mix religion with politics Religious tolerance and fraternity are basic features and postulates of the Constitution as a scheme for national integration and sectional or religious unity Programmes or principles evolved by political parties based on religion amounts to recognising religion as a part of the political governance which the Constitution expressly prohibited it.	None
It violates the basic features of the Constitution.	None
Positive secularism negates such a policy and any action in furtherance thereof would be violative of the basic features of the Constitution.	None
Any act done by a political party or the Government of the State run by that party in furtherance of its programme or policy would also be in violation of the Constitution and the law.	None
When the President receives a report from a Governor or otherwise had such information that the Government of the State is not being carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution, the President is entitled to consider such report and reach his satisfaction in accordance with law.	None
A person who challenges the Presidential Proclamation must prove strong prima facie case that the Presidential Proclamation is unconstitutional or invalid and not in accordance with law.	None
On the Courts satisfying that the strong prima facie case has been made out and if it is a High Court, it should record reasons before issuing discovery order nisi, summoning the records from the Union of India.	None
The Government is entitled to claim privilege under Section 123 of the Indian Evidence Act and also the claim under Article 74(2) of the Constitution.	None
The Court is to consider the records in camera before taking any further steps in the matter.	None
Article 74(2) is not a barrier for judicial review.	None
It only places limitation to examine whether any advice and if so what advice was tendered by the Council of ministers to the President.	None
Article 74(2) receives only this limited protective canopy from disclosure, but the material on the basis of which the advice.	None
was tendered by the Council of Ministers is subject to judicial scrutiny.	None
The Union of India, when discovery order nisi is Issued by this Court, would act in aid of the Court under Article 142(2) and is enjoined to produce the material, the foundation for action under Art. 356.	None
As held earlier before calling upon the Union to produce the material, the Court must first find strong prima facie case and when the records are produced they are to be considered in camera.	None
Judicial review is a basic feature of the Constitution.	None
This Court High Courts have constitutional duty and responsibility to exercise judicial review as sentinal quevive.	None
Judicial review is not concerned with the merits of the decision, but with the manner in which the decision was taken.	None
The exercise of the power under Article 356 is a constitutional exercise of the power, the normal subjective satisfaction of an administrative decision on objective basis applied by the Courts to administrative decisions by subordinate officers or quasijudicial or subordinate legislation does not apply to the decision of the President under Article 356.	None
Judicial review must be distinguished from the justiciability by the Court.	None
The two concepts are not synonymous.	None
The power of judicial review is a constituent power and cannot be abdicated by judicial process of interpretation.	None
However, justiciability of the decision taken by the President is one of exercise of the power by the Court hedged by selfimposed judicial restraint.	None
It is a cardinal principle of our Constitution that noone, howsoever lofty, can claim to be the sole Judge of the power given under the Constitution.	None
Its actions are within the confines of the powers given by the Constitution.	None
This Court as final arbiter in interpreting the Constitution, declares what the law is.	None
Higher judiciary has been assigned a delicate task to determine what powers the Constitution has conferred on each branch of the Government and whether the actions of that branch transgress such limitations, it is the duty and responsibility of this Court High Court to lay down the law.	None
It is the constitutional duty to uphold the constitutional values and to enforce the constitutional limitations as the ultimate interpreter of the Constitution.	None
The judicial review, therefore, extends to examine the constitutionality of the Proclamation issued by the President under Article 356.	None
It is a delicate task, though loaded with political overtones, to be exercised with circumspection and great care.	None
In deciding finally the validity of the Proclamation, there cannot be any hard and fast rules or fixed set of rules or principles as to when the Presidents satisfaction is justiciable and valid.	None
Justiciability is not a legal concept with a fixed content, nor is it susceptible of scientific verification.	None
Its use is the result of many pressures or variegated reasons.	None
Justiciability may be looked at from the point of view of common sense limitation.	None
Judicial review may be avoided on questions of purely political nature, though pure legal questions camouflaged by the political questions are always justiciable.	None
The Courts must have judicially manageable standards to decide a particular controversy.	None
Justiciability on a subjective satisfaction conferred in the widest terms to the political coordinate executive branch created by the constitutional scheme itself is one of the considerations to be kept in view in exercising judicial review.	None
There is an initial presumption that the acts have been regularly performed by the President.	None
The proviso to Article 74(l) reenforces that on the advice tendered by the Council of Ministers to the President, the latter actively applies his mind and reaches the satisfaction that a situation has arisen in which the Government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
The word otherwise enlarges the width and ambit of satisfaction reached by the President.	None
In some cases such satisfaction lacks judicially manageable standards or resolution.	None
The abuse of the power by the high constitutional functionaries can not be assumed, but must be strictly proved.	None
It also cannot be assumed that the Presidential Proclamation was lightly issued.	None
The exercise of discretionary satisfaction may depend on diverse varied and variegated circumstances.	None
The Constitution confided exercise of the power under Article 356 in the highest executive of the land, the President of India aided and advised by the Council of Ministers at its head by the Prime Minister.	None
The Prime minister and his Council of Ministers are collectively and individually responsible to the Parliament and accountable to the people.	None
Confidence reposed on the highest executive itself is a circumstance to be kept in view in adjudging whether the satisfaction reached by the President is vitiated by law.	None
It is impermissible to attribute bad faith or personal mala fides to the President in the face of constitutional prohibition of answerability by Art. 361.	None
But if the proof of mala fide abuse of power is available, appropriate remedy would be available in the Constitution under Art.	None
61.	None
The decision can be tested on the ground of legal mala fides, or high irrationality in the exercise of the discretion to issue Presidential Proclamation.	None
Therefore, the satisfaction reached by the President for issuing the Proclamation under Article 356 must be tested only on those grounds of unconstitutionality, but not on the grounds that the material which enabled him to reach the satisfaction was not sufficient or inadequate.	None
The traditional parameters of judicial review therefore cannot be extended to the area of exceptional and extraordinary powers exercised under Article 356.	None
The doctrine of proportionality cannot be extended to the power exercised under Article 356.	None
The ultimate appeal over the action of the President is to the electorate and judicial selfrestraint is called in aid, in which event the faith of the people in the efficacy of the judicial review would be strengthened and the judicial remedy becomes meaningful.	None
Under Article 356 as soon as the Proclamation was issued, under subcl.	None
(3) of Article 356, the President shall seek its approval from both Houses of Parliament within two months from the date of its issue unless it is revoked in the meanwhile.	None
A consistent constitutional convention has been established that on issuing the Proclamation the President on his assumption of the functions of the Government of the State directs the Governor to exercise all the executive functions of the Government of the State with the aid and advice of the appointed Advisors.	None
He declares that the power of the Legislature of the State shall be exercisable by or under the authority of the Parliament and makes incidental and consequential provisions necessary to give effect to the object of proclamation by suspending whole or any part of the operation of any provision of the Constitution relating to any body or authority of the State which includes dissolution of the Legislative Assembly and removal of the State Government.	None
The Parliament exercises the legislative power thereon under Article 357 and in turn it confers on the President the powers relating to entries in List II of the VII Schedule.	None
The Governor of the State with the aid and advice of the advisors exercise the executive functions on behalf of the President.	None
The convention attained the status of law.	None
This consistent law has been operating without any constitutional hiatus.	None
Granting of stay of operation of Presidential Proclamation creates constitutional and administrative hiatus and incongruity.	None
The Union and the State simultaneously cannot operate the legislative and executive powers in List II of Schedule 7 of the Constitution.	None
Thereby the simultaneous bicameral functions by the Union and the State is an anathema to the democratic principle and constitutional scheme.	None
It would lead to incongruity and incompatibility.	None
There is no.	None
express provision in the Constitution to revive the Assembly dissolved under the Presidential Proclamation or to reinduct the removed Government of the State.	None
In interpreting the Constitution on the working of the democratic institutions set under the Constitution, it is impermissible to fill the gaps or to give directions to revive the dissolved Assembly and to reinduct the dismissed Government of the State into office.	None
Equally stay cannot be granted of the operation of the Presidential Proclamation till both Houses of Parliament approve the Presidential Proclamation.	None
The suspension without dissolution of the legislative Assembly of the State also creates functional disharmony leading to constitutional crisis.	None
The grant of stay of elections to the Legislative Assembly, occasioned pursuant to the Presidential Proclamation, also creates constitutional crisis.	None
Therefore, the courts should not issue such directions leaving it to the Parliament to amend the Constitution if need be.	None
The floor test, may be one consideration which the Governor may keep in view.	None
But whether or not to resort to it would depend on prevailing situation.	None
The possibility of horse trading also to be kept in view having regard to the prevailing political situation.	None
It is not possible to formulate or comprehend a set of rules for the exercise of the power by the Governor to conduct floor test.	None
The Governor should be left free to deal with the situation according to his best judgment keeping in view the Constitution and the conventions of the Parliamentary system of Government.	None
Though Sarkaria Commission and Rajamannar Commission, headed by two distinguished Judges of this land, recommended floor test, it could only mean that that is consideration which it must cross the mind of the Governor.	None
It would be suffice to say that the Governor should be alive to the situation but he would be the sole Judge on the question whether or not conditions are conducive to resort to floor test.	None
The satisfaction reached by the President in issuing Presidential Proclamation and dissolving the Legislative Assemblies of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh cannot be faulted as it was based on the fact of violation of the secular features of the Constitution which itself is a ground to hold that a situation has arisen in which the Government of the concerned States cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Therefore, the satisfaction cannot be said to be unwarranted.	None
The appeals of the Union from the judgment of the Madhya Pradesh High Court is allowed accordingly and the judgment of the High Court is set aside.	None
The dissolution of the Meghalaya Assembly though vulnerable to attack as unconstitutional, it in has become infructuous due to subsequent elections and the newly elected State Legislature and the Government of the State of Meghalaya are functioning thereafter.	None
Therefore, no. futile writs could be issued as the court does not act in vain.	Decision
The appeal of Bommai and the transferred petitions are accordingly dismissed, but in the circumstances without costs.	Decision
P. JEEVAN REDDY, J. (for himself and on behalf of S. C. Agrawal, J.)  200.	None
Article 356 of the Constitution of India is a provision without a parallel.	None
Constitution of no.	None
other country contains a similar provision.	None
The only other Constitution that contains a somewhat similar provision is the Constitution of Pakistan of 1973, viz., Art. 58(2) and Art.	None
112(2).	None
Both the Indian and Pakistani provisions appear to be inspired by S. 45 and S. 93 of the Government of India Act, 1935.	None
Article 356, however, is qualitatively different, while the Pakistani provisions are more akin to the provisions of 1935 Act.	None
Under Art.	None
356, the President is empowered to remove the State Government, dissolve the Legislative Assembly of the State and take over the functions of the Government of the State in case he is satisfied that the Government of that State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
In the context of the Indian Constitution (more specifically after the amendment of Article 74(1) by the 42nd (Amendment) Act) this really is the power vested in the Council of Ministers headed by the Prime Minister at the Centre.	None
The action can be taken either on the report of the Governor or on the basis of information received otherwise or both.	None
An awesome power indeed.	None
The only check envisaged by the Constitution apart from the judicial review  is the approval by both Houses of Parliament which in practice has proved to be ineffective, as this judgment will demonstrate.	None
And with respect to judicial review of the action under Art.	None
356, serious reservations are expressed by the counsel for the Union of India and other respondents.	None
If what they say is accepted, there is a danger of this power eroding the very federal structure of our State and introducing a serious imbalance in our constitutional scheme.	None
It is, therefore, necessary to define the parameters of this power and the parameters of judicial review in these matters in the interest of our constitutional system.	None
It is for this reason that we heard elaborate arguments from all the parties before us on the meaning, scope and dimensions of the power under this Article.	None
We may say, we are fully aware of the delicate nature of the problem.	None
We are aware that though the questions raised herein are constitutional in character, they do have political overtones.	None
It is quite likely that our views will not be found palatable by some but that probably cannot be helped.	None
Sworn to uphold the Constitution, we must say what the Article says and means.	None
It is true that on account of elections having taken place subsequent to the issuance of the proclamations impugned herein, no. effective relief can be granted in these matters, we are yet requested by all the parties concerned herein that we should express ourselves on all the issues arising herein so that the principles enunciated by this court may serve as guidelines for the future for all concerned.	None
ARTICLE 356	None
THE BACKGROUND India became a British colony in the year 1858.	None
Roughly twothirds of it was under direct British rule while the remaining onethird was under the rulership of more than 500 Princes, who in turn were directly under the thumb of the British crown.	None
The 1935 Act Introduced, for the first time, the concept of division of powers between the centre and the provinces.	None
Most of the powers were retained with the centre.	None
The provincial governments were kept under an everwatchful and all powerful centre.	None
The Governors in the provinces and the GovernorGeneral at the centre exercised real and substantial power, unlike the Governors and the President under the Constitution.	None
From the British point of view, it was an experiment, the first one, in selfrule by the Indians.	None
A few powers were entrusted to the elected governments at the centre or in the provinces even those could be resumed and taken back by the GovernorGeneral or Governor, as the case may be, whenever he was satisfied that the government at the centre or of the province could not be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Act.	None
GovernorGeneral and Governor, under the 1935 Act, meant the imperial colonial power.	None
Evidently, the British Parliament was not prepared to trust the Indian political parties.	None
Many of them were opposed to British rule and some of their leaders had declared openly that they would enter the Legislatures and the government with a view to break the system from within.	None
Sections 45 and 93 were the products of this mistrust.	None
But then why was a provision like Art.	None
356 ever made in the Constitution?	None
What was the occasion and necessity for it?	None
For ascertaining this, we may have to turn to the debates in the Constituent Assembly.	None
The draft Arts.	None
277(A) and 278 (corresponding to Arts.	None
355 and 356) were taken up for consideration on August 3, 1949.	None
It would be appropriate to read both Arts.	None
355 and 356 as enacted by the Constituent Assembly Duty of the Union to protect States against external aggression and internal disturbance.	None
It shall be the duty of the Union to protect every State against external aggression and internal disturbance and to ensure that the government of every State is carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution.	None
Provisions in case of failure of constitutional machinery in States.(I) If the President, on receipt of report from the Governor of a State or otherwise, is satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution, the President may by Proclamation (a) assume to himself all or any of the functions of the Government of the State and all or any of the powers vested in or exercisable by the Governor or any body or authority in the State other than the Legislature of the State (b) declare that the powers of the Legislature of the State shall be exercisable by or under the authority of Parliament (c) make such incidental and consequential provisions as appear to the President to be necessary or desirable for giving effect to the objects of the Proclamation, including provisions for suspending in whole or in part the operation of any provisions of this Constitution relating to any body or authority in the State Provided that nothing in this clause shall authorise the President to assume to himself any of the powers vested in or exercisable by a High Court, or to suspend in whole or in part the operation of any provision of this Constitution relating to High Courts.	None
Any such Proclamation may be revoked or varied by a subsequent Proclamation.	None
Every Proclamation issued under this article shall be laid before each House of Parliament and shall, except where it is a Proclamation revoking a previous Proclamation, cease to operate at the expiration of two months unless before the expiration of that period it has been approved by resolutions of both Houses of Parliament Provided that if any such Proclamation (not being a Proclamation revoking a previous Proclamation) is issued at a time when the House of the People is dissolved or the dissolution of the House of the People takes place during the period of two months referred to in this clause, and if a resolution approving the Proclamation has been passed by the Council of States, but no. resolution with respect to such Proclamation has been passed by the House of the People before the expiration of that period, the Proclamation shall cease to operate at the expiration of thirty days from the date on which the House of the People first sits after its reconstitution unless before the expiration of the said period of thirty days a resolution approving the Proclamation has been also passed by the House of the People.	None
A Proclamation so approved shall, unless revoked, cease to operate on the expiration of a period of six months from the date of issue of the Proclamation Provided further that if the dissolution of the House of the People takes place during any such period of six months and a resolution approving the continuance in force of such Proclamation has been passed by the Council of States, but no.	None
resolution with respect to the continuance in force of such Proclamation has been passed by the House of the People during the said period, the Proclamation shall cease to operate at the expiration of thirty days from the date on which the House of the People first sits after its reconstitution unless before the expiration of the said period of thirty days a resolution approving the continuance in force of the Proclamation has been also passed by the House of the People.	None
Dr. B. R. Ambedkar was of the view that the Constitution must provide for situation of breakdown of the constitutional machinery in the States analogous to the provisions contained in S. 93 of the 1935 Act.	None
If a situation arises, for whatever reason, where the government of a State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution, he said, the President of India must be empowered to remedy it.	None
For that purpose, he could take even all or any of the functions of the government as well as of the State Legislature.	None
He could also make such other provisions as he may think necessary  including suspension of the provisions of the Constitution except those relating to High Court.	None
This power, he stated, must be understood in the context of draft Art.	None
277(A)	None
(Art. 355), which cast an obligation upon the Union to protect every State against external aggression and internal disturbance and to ensure that the government of every State is carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
To discharge this obligation, he said, the centre must be empowered to take over the government of the State.	None
At the same time, he said, the President is not expected to act in a wanton or arbitrary manner but on the basis of a report from the Governor or on the basis of other material in his possession, as the case may be.	None
Several members strongly opposed the incorporation of a provision like the one contained in draft Art.	None
278 on the ground inter alia that it would be an invasion upon the field reserved for the States and that permitting the President to take over the government of the State even on the basis of the information received otherwise i.e., Without there being a report of the Governor to that effect, was bound to be abused.	None
A few members pleaded that this power should be exercised only on the report of the Governor and that the words or otherwise should be deleted from the Article.	None
All these objections were overridden by Dr. Ambedkar with the argument that no. provision of any Constitution, for that matter is immune from being abused.	None
He then made this significant statement In fact I share the sentiments expressed by my Honble friend Mr. Gupte yesterday that the proper thing we ought to expect is that such articles will never be called into operation and that they would remain a dead letter.	None
If at all they are brought into operation, I hope the President, who is endowed with these powers, will take proper precaution before actually suspending the administration of the provinces.	None
He added I hope the first thing he will do would be to issue a clear warning to a province that has erred, that things were not happening in the way in which they were intended to happen in the Constitution.	None
Article 356 was thus conceived as a mechanism to ensure that the government of the State is carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Democratic rule based on adult franchise was being introduced for the first time.	None
Almost I 1/3rd of the country, under princely rule, had never known elections.	None
Rule of Law was a novelty in those areas.	None
The infant democracy required careful nurturing.	None
Many a hiccup was expected in the days to come.	None
This perhaps explains the need for a provision like the one in Art.	None
356.	Arguments of Respondent
Article 356 finds place in PartXVIII which carries the heading Emergency Provisions.	None
Article 352, the first article in this Part, empowers the President of India to proclaim emergency in the country or any part thereof if he is satisfied that a grave emergency exists whereby the security of India or any part thereof is threatened whether by war, external aggression or armed rebellion.	None
(By the 44th Amendment, the words armed rebellion were substituted in the place of the words internal disturbance).	None
Articles 353 and 354 set out the effects of such a proclamation and provide for certain incidental matters.	None
Article 355, set out hereinbefore, imposes a duty upon the Union to protect the States agains external aggression and armed rebellion and also to ensure that the government of every State is carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Articles 355, 356 and 357 go together.	None
Article 356 provides for the action to be taken by the President where he is satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the government of a State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution by making a proclamation in that behalf, while Art. 357 sets out the powers that can be exercised by the Parliament when a proclamation under Art.	None
356 is in operation.	None
Articles 358 and 359 deal with suspending of certain fundamental rights during the period the proclamation under Art.	None
352 is in operation, while Art. 360 empowers the President to declare financial emergency in certain situations.	None
In a sense, Article 356 is an emergency provision though, it is true, it is qualitatively different from the emergency contemplated by Art. 352, or for that matter, from the financial emergency contemplated by Art. 360.	None
Undoubtedly, breakdown of the Constitutional machinery in a State does gives rise to a situation of emergency.	None
Emergency means a situation which is not normal, a situation which calls for urgent remedial action.	None
Article 356 confers a power to be exercised by the President in exceptional circumstances to discharge the obligation cast upon him by Art. 355.	None
It is a measure to protect and preserve the Constitution, consistent with his oath.	None
He is as much bound to exercise this power in a situation contemplated by Art.	None
356 as he is bound not to use it where such a situation has not really arisen.	None
By 42nd (Amendment) Act of the Constitution, clause (5) was added in Art.	None
356.	Arguments of Respondent
It was deleted by 44th (Amendment) Act which incorporated an altogether different provision as clause (5).	None
It would be appropriate to take the article as it now stands while trying to understand its meaning, purpose and scope.	None
But before we do that, it would be appropriate to examine the nature of the Indian Federation as ordained by our Constitution.	None
THE FEDERAL NATURE OF THE CONSTITUTION	None
The expression Federation or federal form of government has no. fixed meaning.	None
It broadly Indicates a division of powers between a central (federal) government and the units (States) comprised therein.	None
No. two federal constitutions are alike.	None
Each of them, be it of U.S.A., Canada, Australia or of any other country, has its own distinct character.	None
Each of them is the culmination of certain historical process.	None
So is our constitution.	None
It is, therefore, futile to try to ascertain and fit our constitution into any particular mould.	None
It must be understood in the light of our own historical process and the constitutional evolution.	None
One thing is clear it was not a case of independent States coming together to form a Federation as in the case of U.S.A. A review of the provisions of the Constitution shows unmistakably that while creating a federation, the founding fathers wished to establish a strong centre.	None
In the light of the past history of this subcontinent, this was probably a natural and necessary decision.	None
A land as varied as India is a strong centre is perhaps a necessity.	None
This bias towards centre is reflected in the distribution of legislative heads between the Centre and States.	None
All the more important heads of Legislation are placed in ListI. Even among the legislative heads mentioned in ListII, several of them, e.g., Entries 2, 13, 17, 23, 24, 26, 27, 32, 33, 50, 57 and 63 are either limited by or made subject to certain Entries in ListI to some or the other extent.	None
Even in the concurrent list (ListIII), the Parliamentary enactment is given the primacy, irrespective of the fact whether such enactment is earlier or later in point of time to a State enactment on the same subjectmatter.	None
Residuary powers are with the Centre.	None
By the 42nd Amendment, quite a few of the Entries in ListII were omitted and or transferred to other lists.	None
Above all, Article 3 empowers the Parliament to form new States out of existing States either by merger or division as also to increase, diminish or alter the boundaries of the States.	None
In the process, existing States may disappear and new ones may come into existence.	None
As a result of the Reorganisation of States Act, 1956, fourteen States and six Union Territories came into existence in the place of twenty seven States and one area.	None
Even the names of the States can be changed by the Parliament unilaterally.	None
The only requirement, in all this process, being the one prescribed in the proviso to Art. 3, viz., ascertainment of the views of the Legislatures of the affected States.	None
There is single citizenship, unlike U.S.A. The judicial organ, one of the three organs of the State is one and single for the entire country again unlike U.S.A., where you have the Federal judiciary and State judiciary separately.	None
Articles 249 to 252 further demonstrate the primacy of Parliament.	None
If the Rajya Sabha passes a resolution by 2/3rd majority that in the national interest, Parliament should make laws with respect to any matter in ListII.	None
Parliament can do so (Art. 249), no. doubt, for a limited period.	None
During the operation of a proclamation of emergency, Parliament can make laws with respect to any matter in List11 (Art. 250).	None
Similarly, the Parliament has power to make laws for giving effect to International Agreements (Art. 253).	None
So far as the finances are concerned, the States again appear to have been placed in a less favourable position, an aspect which has attracted a good amount of criticism at the hands of the States and the proponents of the States autonomy.	None
Several taxes are collected by the Centre and made over, either partly or fully, to the States.	None
Suffice it to say that Centre has been made far more powerful visavis the States.	None
Correspondingly, several obligations too are placed upon the Centre including the one in Art.	None
355  the duty to protect every State against external aggression and internal disturbance.	None
Indeed, this very Article confers greater power upon the Centre in the name of casting an obligation upon it, viz., to ensure that the Government of every State is carried on, in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution.	None
It is both a responsibility and a power.	None
The fact that under the scheme of our Constitution, greater power is conferred upon the Centre visavis the States does not mean that States are mere appendages of the Centre.	None
Within the sphere allotted to them, States are supreme.	None
The Centre cannot tamper with their powers.	None
More particularly, the Courts should not adopt an approach, an interpretation, which has the effect of or tends to have the effect of whittling down the powers reserved to the States.	None
It is a matter of common knowledge that over the last several decades, the trend the world over is towards strengthening of Central Governments be it the result of advances in technological scientific fields or otherwise, and that even in U.S.A. the Centre has become far more powerful notwithstanding the obvious bias in that Constitution in favour of the States.	None
All this must put the Court on guard against any conscious whittling down of the powers of the States.	None
Let it be said that the federalism in the Indian Constitution is not a matter of administrative convenience, but one of principle the outcome of our own historical process and a recognition of the ground realities.	Reasoning
This aspect has been dealt with elaborately by Sri M. C. Setalvad in his Tagore Law Lectures Union and State relations under the Indian Constitution (published by Eastern Law House, Calcutta, 1974).	None
The nature of the Indian federation with reference to its historical background, the distribution of legislative powers, financial and administrative relations, powers of taxation, provisions relating to trade, commerce and industry, have all been dealt with analytically.	None
It is not possible  nor is it necessary  for the present purposes to refer to them.	None
It is enough to note that our Constitution has certainly a bias towards Centre visavis the States (Automobile Transport (Rajasthan) Ltd. v. State of Rajasthan.	None
It is equally necessary to emphasise that Courts should be careful not to upset the delicately crafted constitutional scheme by a process of interpretation.	None
A few decisions supporting the view expressed hereinabove May be referred to briefly.	None
In Berubari Union and Exchange of Enclaves  Reference under Art.	None
143 1959 Gajendragadkar, J. observed It may, therefore, be assumed that in construing Art.	None
3 we should take into account the fact that the Constitution contemplated changes of the territorial limits of the constituent States and there was no. guarantee about their territorial integrity.	None
Similarly, in State of West Bengal v. Union of India at p. 405 at p. 1255), this Court observed There is no. constitutional guarantee against alteration of the boundaries of the States.	None
By Art. 2 of the Constitution the Parliament may admit into the Union or establish new States on such terms and conditions as it thinks fit, and by Art. 3 the Parliament is by law authorised to form a new State by redistribution of the territory of a State or by uniting any territory to a part of any State, increase the area of any State, diminish the area of any State, alter the boundaries of any State, and alter the name of any State.	None
Legislation which so vitally affects the very existence of the States may be moved on the recommendation of the President which in practice means the recommendation of the Union Ministry, and if the proposal in the Bill affects the area, boundaries or name of any of the States, the President has to refer the Bill to the Legislature of that State for merely expressing its views thereon.	None
Parliament is therefore by law invested with authority to alter the boundaries of any State and to diminish its area so as to destroy a State with all its powers and authority.	None
AN ANALYSIS OF ARTICLE 356	None
The heading of Article 356 characterises it as a provision providing for failure of Constitutional machinery in States.	None
Cl.(1), however, does not use the words failure of constitutional machinery.	None
Even so, the significance of the title of the section cannot be overlooked.	None
It emphasises the level, the stage, the situation in which the power is to be exercised.	None
Clause (1) speaks of the President being satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution.	None
If so satisfied, he may, by proclamation, assume and exercise the several powers mentioned in subclauses (a), (b) and (c).	None
An analysis of clause (1) of the Article yields the following ingredients (a) if the President is satisfied (b) on receipt of report from the Governor of State or other wise (c) that a situation has arisen in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution (d) the President may by proclamation, (i) assume to himself all or any of the functions of the Government of the State or all or any of the powers of the Governor or any other body or authority in the State except the legislature of the State (ii) declare that the powers of the legislature of the State shall be exercised by the Parliament or under its authority and (iii) make such incidental or consequential provisions as appear to him to be necessary or desirable for giving effect to the objects of the proclamation including provisions for suspending in whole or in part the operation of any provisions of this Constitution relating to any body or authority in the State.	None
(The proviso to clause (i) clarifies that nothing in the said clause shall authorise the President to assume to himself any of the powers vested in or exercisable by a High Court or to suspend in whole or part the operation of any provision relating to High Courts.)	None
Clause(2) says that any proclamation under cl.	None
(1) can be revoked or varied by a subsequent proclamation.	None
Clause (3) provides that every proclamation issued under cl.	None
(1) (except a proclamation revoking a previous proclamation) shall be laid before each House of the Parliament and shall cease to operate at the expiration of two months unless before the expiration of that period it has been approved by resolutions of both Houses of Parliament.	None
The proviso to clause (3) provides for a situation where the Lok Sabha is dissolved on the date of the proclamation or is dissolved within two months of such proclamation.	None
Clause (4) says that a proclamation so approved by both Houses of Parliament shall unless revoked earlier, cease to operate on the expiration of period of six months.	None
(By 42nd Amendment, the words one year were substituted for the words six months but by 44th Amendment, the words six months have been restored).	None
The three provisos to clause (4) provide for certain situations which it is not necessary for us to consider for the purpose of these cases.	None
Clause (5), as inserted by 38th Amendment ran as follows (5) Notwithstanding any thing in this Constitution, the satisfaction of The President mentioned in clause (1) shall be final and conclusive and shall not be questioned in any court on any grounds.	Arguments of Petitioner
By 44th Amendment, however, this clause was repealed altogether and in its place a new clause (5) introduced which limits the maximum period, for which such a proclamation can be operative, to one year except in a case where a proclamation of emergency is in operation.	None
It is not necessary to consider clause (5) also for the purpose of these cases.	None
The power conferred by Article 356 is a conditioned power it is not an obsolute power to be exercised in the discretion of the President.	None
The condition is the formation of satisfaction subjective, no. doubt  that a situation of the type contemplated by the clause has arisen.	None
This satisfaction may be formed on the basis of the report of the Governor or on the basis of other information received by him or both.	None
The existence of relevant material is a precondition to the formation of satisfaction.	None
The use of the word may indicates not only a discretion but an obligation to consider the advisability and necessity of the action.	None
It also involves an obligation to consider which of the several steps specified in subclauses (a), (b) and (c) should be taken and to what extent?	None
The dissolution of the Legislative Assembly assuming that it is permissible  is not a matter of course.	None
It should be resorted to only when it is necessary for achieving the purposes of the proclamation.	None
The exercise of the power is made subject to approval of the both Houses of Parliament.	None
Clause (3) is both a check on the power and a safeguard against abuse of power.	None
Clause (1) Clause (1) opens with the words if the Presidentis satisfied.	None
These words are indicative of the satisfaction being a subjective one.	Facts
In Barium Chemicals v. Company Law Board,  a decision followed uniformly ever since it was pronounced Shelat, J. pointed out, on a consideration of several English and Indian authorities that the expressions is satisfied, is of the opinion, or has reasons to believe are indicative of subjective satisfaction, though it is true the nature of the power has to be determined on a totality of consideration of all relevant provisions.	None
Indeed, there was no. controversy before us regarding the nature of this power.	None
Clause (1), it may be noted, uses the words is satisfied, which indicates a more definite state of mind than is indicated by the expressions is of the opinion or has reasons to believe.	None
Since it is a case of subjective satisfaction, question of observing the principles of natural justice does not and cannot arise.	None
Having regard to the nature of the power and the situation in which it is supposed to be exercised, principles of natural justice cannot be imported into the clause.	None
It is evident that the satisfaction has to be formed by the President fairly, on a consideration of the report of the Governor and or other material, if any, placed before him.	None
Of course, the President under our Constitution being, what may be called, a constitutional President obliged to act upon the aid and advice of the council of ministers (which aid and advice is binding upon him by virtue of clause (1) of Art. 74), the satisfaction referred to in Art.	None
356(l) really means the satisfaction of the union council of ministers with the Prime Minister at its head.	None
Clause (1) requires the President to be satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution .	None
The words cannot emphasise the type of situation contemplated by the clause.	None
These words read with the title of the Article provisions in case or failure of constitutional machinery in States emphasise the nature of the situation contemplated.	None
The words provisions of this Constitution mean what they say.	None
The said words cannot be limited or confined to a particular chapter in the Constitution or to a particular set of Articles.	None
While construing a constitutional provision, such a limitation ought not to be ordinarily inferred unless the context does clearly so require.	None
The provisions of the Constitution include the chapter relating to fundamental rights, the chapter relating to directive principles of the State policy as also the preamble to the Constitution.	None
Though, at one time, it was thought that preamble does not form part of the Constitution, that view is no. longer extent.	None
It has been laid by the majority of Judges in Keshavananda Bharti v. State of Kerala, 1973 Suppl SCR I that preamble does form part of the Constitution.	None
It cannot be otherwise.	None
The attempt to limit the said words to certain machinery provisions in the Constitution is misconceived and cannot be given effect to.	None
It is difficult to believe that the said words do not take in fundamental provisions like the fundamental rights in ChapterIII.	None
It must, however, be remembered that it is not each and every noncompliance with a particular provision of the Constitution that calls for the exercise of the power under Art. 356(l).	None
The noncompliance or violation of the Constitution should be such as to lead to or give rise to a situation where the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
It is indeed difficult  nor is it advisable  to catalogue the various situations which may arise and which would be comprised within clause (1).	None
It would be more appropriate to deal with concrete cases as and when they arise.	None
The satisfaction of the President referred to in clause (1) may be formed either on the receipt of the report(s) of the Governor or otherwise.	None
The Governor of a State is appointed by the President under Art. 155.	None
He is indeed a part of tile government of tile State.	None
The executive power of the State is vested in him and is exercised by him directly or through officers subordinate to him in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution (Article 154).	None
All executive action of the government of a State is expressed to be taken in the name of the Governor, except a few functions which he is required to exercise in his discretion.	None
He has to exercise his powers with the aid and advice of the council of ministers with the Chief Minister at its head (Article 163).	None
He takes the oath, prescribed by Art. 159, to preserve, protect and defend the Constitution and the laws to the best of his ability.	None
It is this obligation which requires him to report to the President the commissions and omissions of the government of his State which according to him are creating or have created a situation where the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
In fact, it would be a case of his reporting against his own government but this may be a case of his wearing two hats, one as the head of the State government and the other as the holder of an independent constitutional office whose duty it is to preserve, protect and defend the Constitution (See Shamsher Singh v.
