State of Punjab, Since he cannot himself take any action of the nature contemplated by Art. 356(l), he reports the matter to the President and it is for the President to be satisfied whether on the basis of the said report or on the basis of any other information which he may receive otherwise  that situation of the nature contemplated by Art. 356(l) has arisen.	None
It is then and only then that he can issue the proclamation.	None
Once the proclamation under Art. 356(l) is issued or simultaneously with it, the President can take any or all the actions specified in clauses (a), (b) and (c).	Reasoning
Power of the President to dissolve Legislative Assembly of the State We shall now examine whether clause (1) of Article 356 empowers the President to dissolve the Legislative Assembly of the State.	Issue
There are two points of view  which we may set out before expressing our preference ONE VIEW, which is supported by the opinions of some of the learned Judges in State of Rajasthan v. Union of India, is that the power of dissolution is implicit in subclause (a).	None
The reasoning runs thus the President assumes the functions of the government of the State as well as the powers of the Governor under the said subclause the Legislative Assembly can be dissolved by the Governor under Article 174(2)(b) of course, this may have to be done on the advice of the council of ministers with the Chief Minister as its head since the President assumes to himself the powers and functions of both the government and the Governor, he can dissolve the Legislative Assembly as part of the same proclamation or by a subsequent order.	None
THE OTHER VIEW, which says that the President has no.	None
such power, runs along the following lines The clause does not speak of dismissal of the government or the dissolution of the Legislative Assembly.	None
It says that if the President is satisfied that a situation has arisen in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution, the President may (i) assume to himself all or any of the functions of the government of the State (ii) assume to himself all or any of the powers vested in or exercisable by the Governor (iii) assume to himself all or any of the functions of any body or authority in the State other than the Legislature of the State, (iv) declare that the powers of the Legislature of the State shall be exerciseable by or under the authority of the Parliament and (v) make such incidental or consequential provision, as may be necessary for giving effect to the proclamation including suspending in whole or part the operation of any provisions of the Constitution relating to any body or authority in the State except the High Court.	None
Now, when subcl.	None
(a) speaks of the President assuming to himself all or any of the powers vested in or exercisable by the Governor, it surely does not mean or imply dismissal or removal of the Governor.	None
Similarly, the assuming by the President of all or any of the functions or, powers of any body or authority in the State (other than the Legislature of the State) does not mean the dismissal or dissolution of such body or authority.	None
For the same reason, it must be held that the words the President may assume to himself all or any of the functions of the government of the State in subclause (a) do not by themselves mean the dismissal of the State Government.	None
But if these words are read along with the main limb of clause (i) which speaks of a situation in which the government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution, it can and does mean dismissal of the government for the reason that government of the State is carried on by the government of the State alone.	None
This dismissal is not absolute in the sense of a physical death of a living being.	None
It only means putting the government out of the way.	None
Such dismissal does not preclude the President from restoring the government after the period of proclamation is over, or at any time earlier by revoking the proclamation if he is so advised.	None
Coming to subcl.	None
(b), when it speaks of the powers of Legislature of the State being madeexercisable by Parliament, or under its authority, it cannot and does not mean or imply dissolution of the Legislature of the State.	None
It is significant to note that the subclause refers to Legislature of the State andnot Legislative Assembly.	None
In a given State, the Legislature may consist of Legislative Assembly as well as Legislative Council.	None
In such a case, there can be no. question of dissolving the Legislative Council since it is a continuing body (Article 172(3)).	None
Only the Legislative Assembly can be dissolved (Article 174(2)(b)).	None
In other words, there can be no. question of dissolution of the Legislature of the State  the expression employed in subcl.	None
(b).	None
The question may then arise, why was subcl.	None
(b) put in and what does it imply?	None
The answer must be that when the government of the State is dismissed or removed from office, the Legislative Assembly cannot function normally.	None
It is difficult to visualise a Legislative Assembly, or for that matter Legislature, functioning without a council of ministers, i.e., government.	None
Thus, where the government of a State is dismissed or removed from the office, the Legislature of the State becomes ipso facto unworkable.	None
It is for this reason that subcl.	None
(b) provides that the powers of the Legislature of the State shall be exercisable by or under the authority of the Parliament.	None
Indeed, the very fact that clause (b) has provided for only one situation (viz., the powers of the Legislature being vested in the Parliament) means and implies that any other step like dissolution of the Legislative Assembly was not within the contemplation of the Constitutionmakers.	None
Subcl.	None
(c) empowers the President to make such incidental or consequential provisions as may appear to be necessary or desirable for giving effect to the objects of the proclamation.	None
Such incidental or consequential provisions may also include suspending in whole or part the operation of any provisions of this Constitution relating to any body or authority except, of course, the High Court.	None
The provisions of the Constitution relating to the Legislative Assembly of the State may be suspended under subcl.	None
(c) during the period of proclamation  generally referred to as keeping the Legislative Assembly under suspended animation  to prevent the majority party (or any other party) calling upon the Governor to invite it to form the ministry and or for preventing the Legislature from passing resolutions or transacting other business which may interfere with the Presidents rule in the State.	None
It is significant to notice in this connection that during the Constituent Assembly debates on these Articles, Dr. Ambedkar only spoke of suspension of the power of the Legislatures and not their dissolution.	None
(vide page 134  Vol.	None
IX  Constituent Assembly Debates.)	None
According to this line of reasoning since the Legislature of the State can only be kept under suspended animation by suspending the relevant provisions of the Constitution  the Legislature of the State springs back to life with the expiry of the period of proclamation.	None
This is for the reason that with the expiry of the period of proclamation or on the revocation of the proclamation, as the case may be, the suspension of the provisions of the Constitution will also come to an end.	None
The proponents of this view criticize the other (first) view on several grounds firstly, they say, it does not seem to take into consideration the fact that dissolution of the Legislative Assembly is an extremely serious step if this power was supposed to be conferred on the President under cl.	None
(i) of Art.	None
356	Arguments of Petitioner
, the Constitutionmakers would have said so expressly and not left it to be inferred.	None
Secondly, it ignores the language of subcl.	None
(b).	None
Subcl.	None
(b) speaks of powers of the Legislature of the State being exercised by the Parliament or under its authority.	None
Clause (b) does not speak of dissolution of Legislature of the State, since that is an impossibility  only the Legislative Assembly can be dissolved and not the Legislative Council as explained hereinabove.	None
There are quite a few States where the Legislature consists of Legislative Assembly as well as Legislative Council.	None
Thirdly, cl.	None
(1) speaks of failure of the government and not of the Legislative Assembly, though it is true, the government is drawn from and very often forms the majority party in the Legislative Assembly.	None
But the Legislative Assembly also consists of the opposition and other parties, groups and independent members, who may themselves have been pointing out and remonstrating against the unconstitutional working of the government.	None
There does not appear to be any good reason why the Legislative Assembly should be dissloved for the acts and defaults of the government.	None
It is true, say the proponents of this view, if the President cannot dissolve the Legislative Assembly, it would spring back to life after the period of proclamation and elect the very same government which was dismissed.	None
They answer it by saying firstly that this may or may not happen.	None
Secondly, they say, even if the same government is elected again, it is in no. way contrary to the spirit of the Articles.	None
The objection was not to its existence but to its working.	None
There is no. reason to presume that it will again carry on the government otherwise than in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
Having given our anxious consideration to both the contending view points  and notwithstanding the obvious appeal of the second point of view  we are inclined to agree with the first view which says that cl.	None
(i) does empower the President to dissolve the Legislative Assembly.	None
This view is also supported by the decision in State of Rajasthan (besides the fact that over the last fortyfour years, the said power has never been questioned.	None
We are inclined to hold that the power to dissolve the Legislative Assembly is implicit in subclause (a) of clause (1) though there is no.	None
such thing as dissolution of the Legislature of the State where it consists of two Houses.	None
It must also be recognised that in certain situations, dissolution of Legislative Assembly may be found to be necessary for achieving the purposes of the proclamation.	None
Power there is.	None
Its exercise is a different matter.	None
The existence of power does not mean that dissolution of Legislative Assembly should either be treated as obligatory or should invariably be ordered whenever a government of the State is dismissed.	None
It should be a matter for the President to consider, taking into consideration all the relevant facts and circumstances, whether the Legislative Assembly should also be dissolved or not.	None
If he thinks that it should be so dissolved, it would be appropriate, indeed highly desirable, that he states the reasons for such extraordinary step in the order itself.	None
The question then arises at what stage should he exercise this.	None
To answer this query, we must turn to clause (3).	None
Clause (3) says that every proclamation issued under Art. 356(l) shall be laid before both Houses of Parliament and shall cease to operate at the expiry of two months unless before the expiration of that period it has been approved by resolutions passed by both Houses.	None
This is conceived both as a check upon the power and as a vindication of the principle of Parliamentary supremacy over the Executive.	None
The Presidents action which is really the action of the Union Council of Ministers is subject to approval of both Houses of Parliament.	None
Unless approved by both Houses of Parliament, the proclamation lapses at the end of two months and earlier if it is disapproved or declined to be approved by both the houses of Parliament, as explained hereinafter.	None
Having regard to the incongruity of the Executive (even though Union Executive) dissolving the Legislature (even if of a State), it would be consistent with the scheme and spirit of the Constitution  particularly in the absence of a specific provision in the Constitution expressly empowering the President to do so  to hold that this power of dissolution can be exercised by the President only after both Houses of Parliament approve the proclamation and not before such approval.	None
Once the Parliament places its seal of approval on the proclamation, further steps as may be found necessary to achieve the purposes of the proclamation, i.e., dissolution of Legislative Assembly, can be ordered.	None
In other words, once the Parliament approves the initial exercise of his power, i.e., his satisfaction that a situation had arisen where the government of the State could not be carried on in accordance with the Constitution, the President can go ahead and take further steps necessary for effectively achieving the objects of the proclamation.	None
Until the approval, he can only keep the Assembly under suspended animation but shall not dissolve it.	None
It must be made clear even at this stage that while no. writ petition shall be entertained by any court before the actual issuance of proclamation under clause (1), it shall be open to a High Court or Supreme Court to entertain a writ petition questioning the proclamation if it is satisfied that the writ petition raises arguable questions with respect to the validity of the proclamation.	None
The court would be entitled to entertain such a writ petition even before the approval of the proclamation by the Parliament as also after such approval.	None
In an appropriate case and if the situation demands, the High Court Supreme Court can also stay the dissolution of the Assembly but not in such a manner as to allow the Assembly to continue beyond its original term.	None
But in every such case where such an order is passed the High Court Supreme Court shall have to dispose of the matter within two to three months.	None
Not disposing of the writ petition while granting such an interim order would create several complications because the life of the proclamation does not exceed six months even after the approval by Parliament and in any event the proclamation cannot survive beyond one year except in the situation contemplated by clause (5) which is, of course, an exceptional situation.	None
Meaning of approval in clause (3) In State of Rajasthan, Chandrachud, Bhagwati and A. C. Gupta, JJ. have expressed the view that the proclamation issued under clause (i) remains in operation for a period of two months in any event.	None
It is held that even if the Parliament disapproves or declines to approve the proclamation with the said period of two months, the proclamation continues to be valid for two months.	None
The approval of the Parliament under cl.	None
(3) is held to be relevant only for the purpose of continuance of the proclamation beyond two months.	None
It has also been held further that even if both the Houses do not approve or disapprove the proclamation, the government which has been dismissed or the Assembly which may have been dissolved do not revive.	None
With utmost respect to the learned Judges, we find outselves unable to agree with the said view in so far as it says that even where both Houses of Parliament disapprove or do not approve the proclamation, the government which has been dismissed does not revive.	None
(The State of Rajasthan also holds that such disapproval or nonapproval does not revive the Legislative Assembly which may have been dissolved but we need not deal with this aspect since according to the view expressed by us hereinabove, no.	None
such dissolution is permissible before the approval of both the Houses.)	None
Clause (3), it may be emphasised, uses the words approved by resolutions of both Houses of Parliament.	None
The word approval means affirmation of the action by a higher or superior authority.	None
In other words the action of the President has to be approved by the Paliament.	None
The expression approval has an intrinsic meaning which cannot be ignored.	None
Disapproval or nonapproval means that the Houses of Parliament are saying that the Presidents action was not justified or warranted and that it shall no. longer continue.	None
In such a case, the proclamation lapses, i.e., ceases to be in operation at the end of two months  the necessary consequence of which is the status quo ante revives.	None
To say that notwithstanding the disapproval or nonapproval, the status quo ante does not revive is to rob the concept of approval of its content and meaning.	None
Such a view renders the check provided by clause (3) ineffective and of no. significance whatsoever.	None
The Executive would be telling the Parliament I have dismissed the government.	None
Now, whether you approve or disapprove is of no. consequence because the government in no.	None
event be revived.	None
The deed is done.	None
You better approve it because you have practically no. choice.	None
We do not think that such a course is consistent with the principle of Parliamentary supremacy and Parliamentary control over the Executive, the basic premise of the Parliamentary supremacy.	None
It would indeed mean supremacy of the Executive over the Parliament.	None
The dismissal of a government under subclause (a) of clause (i) cannot also be equated to the physical death of a living being.	None
There is no. irrevocability about it.	None
It is capable of being revived and it revives.	None
Legislative Assembly which may have kept in suspended animation also springs back to life.	None
So far as the validity of the acts done, orders passed and laws, if any, made during the period of operation of the proclamation is concerned, they would remain unaffected inasmuch as the disapproval or nonapproval does not render the proclamation Invalid with retrospective effect.	None
It may be recalled that the power under Art.	Arguments of Petitioner
356(l) is the power vested in the President subject no. doubt to approval within two months.	None
The nonapproval means that the proclamation ceases to be in operation at the expiry of two months, as held in State of Rajasthan ( Now, coming to the power of the court to restore the government to office in case it finds the proclamation to be unconstitutional, it is, in our opinion, beyond question.	None
Even in case the proclamation is approved by the Parliament it would be open to the court to restore the State Government to its office in case it strikes down the proclamation as unconstitutional.	None
If this power were not conceded to the court, the very power of judicial review would be rendered nugatory and the entire exercise meaningless.	None
If the court cannot grant the relief flowing from the invalidation of the proclamation, it may as well decline to entertain the challenge to the proclamation altogether.	None
For, there is no. point in the court entertaining the challenge, examining it, calling upon the Union Government to produce the material on the basis of which the requisite satisfaction was formed and yet not give the relief.	None
In our considered opinion, such a course is inconceivable.	None
A question may arise  what happens to the acts done, orders made and laws enacted by Parliament or under its authority during the period the proclamation was in operation in case the proclamation is declared to be unconstitutional by the court?	None
would all of them become unconstitutional or void?	None
Firstly, there is no. reason to presume that a court which strikes down the proclamation would not provide for this contingency.	None
It would be within the power of the court to say that these acts and orders are saved.	None
Indeed, it should say so in the interests of general public and to avoid all kinds of complications, leaving it to government and the Legislature of the State concerned to rectify, modify or repeal them, if they so choose.	None
The theory of factum valet may also be available to save the acts, orders and things done by the President or under his authority during the said period.	None
It was suggested by Sri Ram Jethmalani that the President can assume all or any of the functions of the State Government without dismissing the government.	None
Emphasis is laid upon the words all or any in subclause (i).	None
In particular, he submitted, where the State Government is found remiss in performing one or some of the functions, that or those functions of the State Government can be assumed by the President with a view to remedy the situation.	None
After rectifying the situation, the counsel submitted, the President will give those functions back to the State Government and that in such a situation there would be no. occasion or necessity for dismissing the State Government.	None
The learned counsel gave the analogy of a motor car  if one or a few of the parts of a car malfunction or cease to function, one need not throw away the car.	None
That or those particular parts can be replaced or rectified and the car would function normally again.	None
It is difficult to agree with the said interpretation.	None
The power under Art. 356(l) can be exercised only where the President is satisfied that the Government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	Reasoning
The title to the Article failure of constitutional machinery in the States also throws light upon the nature of the situation contemplated by it.	None
It means a situation where the government of the State, and not one or a few functions of the government  cannot be carried on in accordance with the Constitution.	None
The inability or unfitness aforesaid may arise either on account of the nonperformance or malperformance of one or more functions of the government or on account of abuse or misuse of any of the powers, duties and obligations of the government.	None
A proclamation under Art. 356(l) necessarily contemplates the removal of the government of the State since it is found unable or unfit to carry on the government of the State in accordance with the provisions of the constitution.	None
In our considered opinion, it is not possible to give effect to the argument of Sri Ram Jethmalani.	None
Acceptance of such an argument would introduce the concept of two governments in the same sphere  the Central Government exercising one or some of the powers of the State Government and the State Government performing the rest.	None
Apart from its novelty, such a situation, in our opinion, does not promote the object underlying Art.	None
356	Arguments of Petitioner
nor is it practicable.	None
Sri Jethmalani brought to our notice the British Joint Parliamentary Report, Para 109, in support of his contention aforementioned.	None
We are unable to see any relevance of the said para to the interpretation of Art. 356(l).	None
Under the Government of India Act, 1935, the GovernorGeneral and the Governor were not constitutional heads of State as under the Constitution.	None
They exercised real power in their own right.	None
Only a few powers were entrusted to the elected governments and even those could be taken away (by the GovernorGeneral at the Centre and the Governor in the provinces) as and when they were satisfied that a situation has arisen where the government at the centre or of the province cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the said Act.	None
Under Art.	None
356	Arguments of Petitioner
, the position is entirely different.	None
The power can be exercised only against the States and that too by the President and not by the Governor.	None
The entire, constitutional philosophy is different.	None
Therefore, merely because the same words all or any in Ss. 93 and 45 of the Government of India Act occur in Art. 356(l), the same, meaning cannot be attributed to them mechanically, ignoring all other factors  assuming that the said words in Ss. 93 and 45 meant what Sri Jethmalani says.	None
ARTICLE 356 IN ACTION Since the commencement of the Constitution, the President has invoked Art.	None
356 on as many as ninety or more occasions.	None
Quite a performance for a provision which was supposed to remain a deadletter.	None
Instead of remaining a deadletter, it has proved to be the deathletter of scores of State Governments and Legislative Assemblies.	None
The Sarkaria Commission which was appointed to look into and report on Centre State relations considered inter alia the manner in which this power has been exercised over the years and made certain recommendations designed to prevent its misuse.	None
Since the Commission was headed by a distinguished Judge of this Court and also because it made its report after an elaborate and exhaustive study of all relevant aspects, its opinions are certainly entitled to great weight notwithstanding the fact that the report has not been accepted so far by the Government of India.	None
In para 6323 the Commission observed that though the words a government of the State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution are of wide amplitude, each and every breach and infraction of constitutional provision, irrespective of its significance, extent and effect, cannot be treated as constituting failure of constitutional machinery.	None
Article 356 the Commission said, provides remedy for a situation where there has been an actual breakdown of the constitutional machinery of the State.	None
Any abuse or misuse of this drastic power, said the Commission, damages the fabric of the Constitution.	None
A literal construction of Art. 356(l) should be avoided, it opined.	None
In para 6401, the Commission noted that failure of constitutional machinery may occur in a number of cases.	None
It setout some of the instances leading to it, viz., (i) political crisis (b) internal subversion (c) fiscal breakdown and (d) noncompliance with constitutional directions of the Union Executive.	None
The Commission, however, hastened to add that the instances set out by it are not claimed to be comprehensive or perfect.	None
Then it examined each of the said four heads separately.	None
In para 6501, the Commission set out illustrations in which invoking Article 356 would be improper.	None
Illustration (iii) in the said paragraph reads thus Where, despite the advice of a duly constituted ministry which has not been defeated on the floor of the house, the Governor decides to dissolve the assembly and without giving the ministry an opportunity to demonstrate its majority through the floortest, recommends its supersession and imposition of Presidents rule merely on subjective assessment that the ministry no. longer commands the confidence of the assembly.	None
In para 6601, the Commission noticed the criticism levelled against the frequent invoking of Article 356 and proceeded to examine its validity.	None
In its opinion, dismissal of nine assemblies following the general elections to the Lok Sabha in March, 1977 and a similar dismissal following the general elections to the Lok Sabha in 1980, were clear instances of invoking Art.	None
356 for purely political purposes unrelated to Art.	None
356.	Arguments of Respondent
After examining the facts and the principle of the decision of this Court in State of Rajasthan v. Union of India and after considering the various suggestions placed before it by several parties, individuals and organisations, the Commission made the following recommendations in para 6.8, which have been strongly commended for our acceptance by the learned counsel for the petitioners.	None
They read as follows Recommendations 6801.	None
Article 356 should be used very sparingly, in extreme cases, as a measure of last resort, when all available alternatives fail to prevent or rectify a breakdown of constitutional machinery in the State.	None
All attempts should be made to resolve the crisis at the State level before taking recourse to the provisions of Art.	None
356.	Arguments of Petitioner
The availability and choice of these alternatives will depend on the nature of the constitutional crisis, its causes and exigencies of the situation.	None
These alternatives may be dispensed with only in cases of extreme urgency where failure on the part of the Union to take immediate action under Art.	None
356 will lead to disastrous consequences.	None
(Paragraph 6704) 6802.	None
A warning should be issued to the errant State, in specific terms, that it is not carrying on the Government of the State in accordance with the Constitution.	None
Before taking action under Art.	None
356, any explanation received from the State should be taken into account.	None
However, this may not be possible in a situation when not taking immediate action would lead to disastrous consequences.	None
(Paragraph 6708) 6803.	None
When an external aggression or internal disturbance paralyses the State administration creating a situation drafting towards a potential breakdown of the Constitutional machinery of the State, all alternative courses available to the Union for discharging its paramount responsibility under Art. 355 should be exhausted to contain the situation.	None
(Paragraph 6317) 6804.	None
(a) In a situation of political breakdown, the Governor should explore all possibilities of having a government enjoying majority support in the Assembly.	None
If it is not possible for such a government to be installed and if fresh elections can be held without avoidable delay, he should ask the outgoing Ministry, if there is one, to continue as a caretaker government, provided the Ministry was defeated solely on a major policy issue, unconnected with any allegations of maladministration or corruption and is agreeable to continue.	None
The Governor should then dissolve the Legislative Assembly, leaving the resolution of the constitutional crisis to the electorate.	None
During the interim period, the caretaker government should be allowed to function.	None
As a matter of convention, the caretaker government should merely carry on the daytoday government and desist from taking any major policy decision.	None
(Paragraph 6408)	None
If the important ingredients described above are absent, it would not be proper for the Governor to dissolve the Assembly and instal a caretaker government.	None
The Governor should recommend proclamation of Presidents rule without dissolving the Assembly.	None
(Paragraph 6409) 6805.	None
Every Proclamation should be placed before each House of Parliament at the earliest, in any case before the expiry of the two months period contemplated in clause (3) of Art. 356.	None
(Paragraph 6713) 6806.	None
The State Legislative Assembly should not be dissolved either by the Governor or the President before the Proclamation issued under Art.	None
356(l) has been laid before Parliament and it has had an opportunity to consider it.	None
Art.	None
356 should be suitably amended to ensure this.	None
(Paragraph 6620) 6807.	None
Safeguards corresponding, in principle, to clauses (7) and (8) of Art. 352 should be incorporated in Art.	None
356 to enable Parliament to review continuance in force of a Proclamation.	None
(Paragraph 6623) 6808.	None
To make the remedy of judicial review on the ground of mala fides a little more meaningful, it should be provided, through an appropriate amendment, notwithstanding anything in clause (2) of Art.	None
74 of the Constitution, the material facts and grounds on which Art. 356(l) is invoked should be made an integral part of the Proclamation issued under that Article.	None
This will also make the control of Parliament over the exercise of this power by the Union Executive, more effective.	None
(Paragraph 6625) 6809.	None
Normally, the President is moved to action under Art.	None
356 on the report of the Governor.	None
The report of the Governor is placed before each House of Parliament.	None
Such a report should be a speaking document containing a precise and clear statement of all material facts and grounds on the basis of which the President may satisfy himself as to the existence or otherwise of the situation contemplated in Art. 356.	None
(Paragraph 6626) 6810.	None
The governors report, on the basis of which a Proclamation under Art.	None
356(l) is issued, should be given wide publicity in all the media and in full.	None
(Paragraph 6628) 6811.	None
Normally, Presidents Rule in a State should be proclaimed on the basis of the Governors report under Art. 356(l).	None
(Paragraph 6629) 6812.	None
In clause (5) of Art.	None
356, the word and occurring between subclauses (a) and (b) should be substituted by or.	None
(Paragraph 6711)	None
The aforesaid recommendations are evidently the outcome of the opinion formed by the Commission that more often than not, the power under Art.	None
356 has been invoked improperly.	None
It is not for us to express any opinion whether this impression of the Commission is justified or not.	None
It is not possible for us to review all the ninety cases in which the said power has been invoked and to say in which cases it was invoked properly and in which cases, not.	None
At the same time, we are inclined to say, having regard to the Constitutional scheme obtaining under our Constitution, that the recommendations do merit serious consideration.	None
It is probably because he was of the opinion that the invocation of this power was not warranted in many cases.	None
Sri P. V. Rajamannar, former Chief Justice of Madras High Court (who was appointed as the Inquiry Committee by the Government of Tamil Nadu to report on the CentreState relations) recommended that Arts.	None
356 and 357 be repealed altogether.	None
(See Para (8) in Chapter IX, Emergency Provisions of his Report, submitted in 1971).	None
In the alternative, he recommended, safeguards must be provided to secure the interest of the States against the arbitrary and unilateral action of a party commanding overwhelming majority at the Centre.	None
In other respects, Sri Rajamannars views accord broadly with the views expressed by the Sarkaria Commission and hence, need not be set out in extenso.	None
THE CONSTITUTION OF INDIA AND THE CONCEPT OF SECULARISM Article 356 (l) speaks of a situation where the Government of a State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	None
We have said hereinbefore that the words the provisions of this Constitution take in all the provisions including the Preamble to the Constitution.	None
The Preamble to the Constitution speaks of a secular Indian Republic.	None
While the respondents counsel contended that secularism being a basic feature of the Constitution, a State Government can be dismissed if it is guilty of unsecular acts, the counsel for petitioners Sri Ram Jethamalani strongly refuted the idea.	None
According to Sri Jethamalani, secularism is a vague concept, not defined in the Constitution and hence, cannot furnish a ground for taking action under Art. 356.	Arguments of Petitioner
Without going into the specifics of the said contention, we shall examine first how far this concept is embedded in our Constitution and in what sense.	None
Having completed the process of framing the Constitution, the Constituent Assembly proceeded to finalise its preamble.	None
Speaking on behalf of and in the name of the people of India, they said, their object has been to constitute India into a Sovereign Democratic Republic, and to secure to all its citizens social justice, liberty of belief, faith and worship, and equality of status and opportunity.	None
They said, the goal was also to promote among all the people of India fraternity assuring the dignity of the individual By the 42nd Amendment to the Constitution, the words socialist, secular were added after the word sovereign and before the word democratic.	None
No. other provision of the Constitution was amended to adumbrate these concepts.	None
Both the expressions  socialist and secularby themselves are not capable of precise definition.	None
We are, however, not concerned with their general meaning of content.	None
Our object is to ascertain the meaning of the expression secular in the context of our Constitution.	None
As the discussion hereafter would demonstrate, the 42nd Amendment merely made explicit what was implicit in it.	None
The preamble speaks of social justice, liberty of belief, faith and worship and of equality of status and of opportunity.	None
Art. 14 (under the subheading Right of Equality) enjoins the State not to deny to any person equality before the law or the equal protection of laws within the territory of India.	None
Arts.	None
15 and 16 elucidate this doctrine of equality.	None
They say that the State shall not discriminate against any citizen on ground only of religion, race or caste, whether in the matter of employment under the State or otherwise.	None
By Art. 25, all persons are declared equally entitled to freedom of conscience and the right to freely profess, practice and propagate religion, subject, of course, to public order, morality and health.	Reasoning
Arts.	None
26, 27 and 28 elucidate the freedom guaranteed by Art. 25.	None
Art.	None
27 declares that no. person shall be compelled to pay any taxes, the proceeds of which are specifically appropriate in payment of expenses for the promotion or maintenance of any particular religion or religious denomination.	None
Art.	None
28(l) decrees that no. religious instruction shall be provided in any educational institution wholly maintained out of the State funds while Art. 28(3) says that no. person attending an educational institution recognised by the State or receiving aid out of State funds shall be required to take part in any religious worship conducted in such institution, except with his or his guardians (in the case of a minor) consent.	None
Similarly, Cl.	None
(2) of Art.	None
30 enjoins upon the State not to discriminate against any educational institution, in granting aid, on the ground that it is under the management of a minority, religious or linguistic.	None
Cl.	None
(3) of Art.	None
51 A (introduced by the 42nd (Amendment) Act) says that it shall be the duty of every citizen of India  to promote harmony and spirit of brotherhood amongst all the people of India transcending religious, linguistic and regional or sectional diversities.	None
What do these articles, read together with the Preamble signify?	None
While Art. 25 of the Constitution guarantees to all its people freedom of religion, Arts. 14, 15 and 16 enjoin upon the State to treat all its people equally irrespective of their religion, caste, faith or belief.	None
While the citizens of this country are free to profess, practice and propagate such religion, faith or belief as they choose, so far as the State is concerned, i.e., from the point of view of the State, the religion, faith or belief of a person is immaterial.	None
To it, all are equal and all are entitled to be treated equally.	None
How is this equal treatment possible, if the State were to prefer or promote a particular religion, race or caste, which necessarily means a less favourable treatment of all other religions, races and castes.	None
How are the Constitutional promises of social justice, liberty of belief, faith or worship and equality of status and opportunity to be attained unless the State eschews the religion, faith or belief of a person from its consideration altogether while dealing with him, his rights, his duties and his entitlements?	None
Secularism is thus more than passive attitude of religious tolerance.	None
It is positive concept of equal treatment of all religions.	None
This attitude is described by some a one of neutrality towards religion or as one of benevolent neutrality.	None
This may be a concept evolved by western liberal thought it may be, as some say, an abiding faith with the Indian people at all points of time.	None
That is not material.	None
What is material is that it is constitutional goal and basic feature of the Constitution as affirmed in Keshvanand Bharati and Indira N Gandhi v. Raj Narain.	None
Any step inconsistent with the constitutional policy is, in plain words, unconstitutional.	None
This does not mean that the State has no. say whatsoever in matters of religion.	None
Laws can be made regulating the secular affairs of Temples, Mosques and others places or worships and maths.	None
(See S. P. Mittal v. Union of India.	None
The power of the Parliament to reform and rationalise the personal laws is unquestioned.	None
The command of Arts.	None
44 is yet to be realised.	None
The correct perspective appeared to have been placed by Sri K. M. Munshi during the Constitutent Assembly Debates.	None
He said Religion must be restricted to spheres which legitimately appertain to religion, and the rest of life must be regulated, unified and modified in such a manner that we may evolve, as early as possible, a strong and consolidated nation.	None
Our first problem and the most important problem is to produce national unity in this country.	None
We think we have got national unity.	None
But there are many factors  and important factors  which still offer serious dangers or our national consolidation, and it is very necessary that the whole of our life, so far as it is restricted to secular spheres, must be unified in such a way that as early as possible, we may be able to say.	None
Well, we are not merely a nation because we say so, but also in effect, by the way we live, by our personal law, we are a strong and consolidated nation.	None
Sri M. C. Setalvad in his lecture on secularism (Patel Memorial Lectures  1965) points out that after affirming the ideas of religious liberty and adequate protection to the minorities at its Karachi Session (1931), the Congress party asserted emphatically that the State shall observe neutrality in regard to all religions.	None
He says that this resolution is in a manner the key to the understanding of the attitude adopted by those who framed the Indian Constitution nearly twenty years later, embodying in it the guarantee of religious neutrality.	None
He also points out that the debates in the Constituent Assembly leave little doubt that what was intended by the Constitution was not the secularisation of the State in the sense of its complete dissociation from religion, but rather an attitude of religious neutrality, with equal treatment to all religions and religious minorities.	None
The same idea is put forward by Gajendragadkar, J., (in his inaugural address to the Seminar on Secularism Its implications for Law and life in India) in the following words It is true that the Indian Constitution does not use the word secularism in any of its provisions, but its material provisions are inspired by the concept of secularism.	None
When it promised all the citizens of India that the aim of the Constitution is to establish socioeconomic justice, it placed before the country as a whole, the ideal of a welfare State.	None
And the concept of welfare is purely secular and not based on any considerations of religion.	None
The essential basis of the Indian Constitution is that all citizens are equal, and this basic equality (guaranteed by Art. 14) obviously proclaims that the religion of a citizen is entirely irrelevant in the matter of his fundamental rights.	None
The State does not owe loyality to any particular religion as such it is not irreligious or antireligion it gives equal freedom for all religions and holds that the religion of the citizen has nothing to do in the matter of socioeconomic problems.	None
That is the essential characteristic of secularism which is writ large in all the provisions of the Indian Constitution.	None
Prof. Upendra Baxi says that Secularism in the Indian Constitution connotes The State by itself, shall not espouse or establish or practice any religion public revenues will not be used to promote any religion the State shall have the power to regulate any economic, financial or other secular activity associated with religious practice (Art.	None
25(2)(a) of the Constitution) the State shall have the power through the law to provide for social welfare and reform or the throwing open of the Hindu religious institutions of a public character to all classes and sections of Hindus (Art. 25(2) (b) of the Constitution) the practice of untouchability (in so far as it may be justified by Hindu religion) is constitutionally outlawed by Art.	None
17 every individual person will have, in that order, an equal right to freedom of conscience and religion these rights are however subject to the power of the State through law to impose restrictions on the ground of public order, morality and health these rights are furthermore subject to other fundamental rights in Part III (The Struggle for the Redefinition of Secularism in India  published in Social Action Vol. 44  January, March, 1994)	None
In short, in the affairs of the State (in its Widest connotation) religion is irrelevant it is strictly a personal affair.	None
In this sense and in this behalf, our Constitution is broadly in agreement with the U.S. Constitution, the First Amendment whereof declares that Congress shall make no.	None
laws respecting an establishment of religion or prohibiting the free exercise thereof (generally referred to as the establishment clause).	None
Perhaps, this is an echo of the doctrine of separation off Church and State may be it is the modern political thought which seeks to separate religion from the State  it matters very little.	None
In this view of the matter, it absolutely erroneous to say that secularism is a vacuous word or a Phantom concept.	None
It is perhaps relevant to point out that our founding fathers read this concept into our Constitution not because it was fashionable to do so, but because it was an imperative in the Indian context.	None
It is true as Sri Ram Jethmalani was at pains to emphasise  that India was divided on the basis of religion and that areas having majority muslim population were constituted into a new entity  Pakistan  which immediately proceeded to proclaim itself as an Islamic Republic, but it is equally a fact that even after partition, India contained a sizeable population of minorities.	None
They comprised not less than 10 to 12 of the population.	None
Inspired by Indian tradition of tolerence and fraternity, for whose sake, the greatest son of Modern India, Mahatma Gandhi, laid down his life and seeking to redeem the promise of religious neutrality held forth by the Congress party, the founding fathers proceeded to create a state, secular in its outlook and egalitarian in its action.	None
They could not have countenanced the idea of treating the minorities as secondclass citizens.	None
On the contrary, the dominant thinking appears to be that the majority community, Hindus, must be secular and thereby help the minorities to become secular.	None
For, it is the majority community alone that can provide the sense of security to others.	None
The significance of the 42nd (Amendment) Act lies in the fact that it formalised the preexisting situation.	None
It put the matter beyond any doubt, leaving room for any controversy.	None
In such a situation, the debate whether the Preamble to the Constitution is included within the words the provisions of this Constitution is really unnecessary.	None
Even if we accept the reading of Sri Jethamalani, Preamble is a key to the understanding of the relevant provisions of the Constitution.	None
The 42nd (Amendment) Act has furnished the key in unmistakble terms.	None
Given the above position, it is clear that if any party or organisation seeks to fight the elections on the basis of a plank which has the proximate effect of eroding the secular philosophy of the Constitution would certainly be guilty of following an unconstitutional course of action.	None
Political parties are formed and exist to capture or share State power.	None
That is their aim.	None
They may be associations of individuals but one cannot ignore the functional relevance.	None
An association of individuals may be devoted to propagation of religion it would be a religious body.	None
Another may be devoted to promotion cultures it would be an cultural organisation .They are not aimed at acquiring State power, whereas a political party does.	None
That is one of its main objectives.	None
This is what we mean by saying functional relevance.	None
One cannot conceive of a democratic form of Government without the political parties.	None
They are part of the political system and constitutional scheme.	None
Nay, they are integral to the governance of a democratic society.	None
If the Constitution requires the State to be secular in thought and action, the same requirement attaches to political parties as well.	None
The Constitution does not recognise it does not permit, mixing religion and State power.	None
Both must be kept apart.	None
That is the constitutional injunction.	None
None can say otherwise so long as this Constitution governs this country.	None
introducing religion into politics is to introduce an impermissible element into body politic and an imbalance in our constitutional system.	None
If a political party espousing a particular religion comes to power, that religion tends to become, in practice, the official religion.	None
All other religions come to acquire a secondary status, at any rate, a less favourable position.	None
This would be plainly antithetical to Arts.	None
14 to 16, 25 and the entire constitutional scheme adumbrated herinabove.	None
Under our Constitution, no. party or organisation can simultaneously be a political and a religious party.	None
It has to be either.	None
Same would be the position, if a party or organisation acts and or behaves by word of mouth, print or in any other manner to bring about the said effect, it would equally be guilty of an act of unconstitutionality.	None
It would have no.	None
right to function as a political party.	None
The fact that party may be entitled to go to people seeking a mandate for a drastic amendment of the Constitution or its replacement by another Constitution is wholly irrelevant in the context.	None
We do not know how the Constitution can be amended so as to remove secularism from the basic structure of the Constitution.	None
Nor do we know how the present Constitution can be replaced by another it is enough for us to know that the Constitution does not provide for such a course  that it does not provide for its own demise.	None
The decision of this Court in Keshavananda Bharti 1973 (S1)	None
SCR 1 at 166 and 280 at Pp. 1536 and 1603) says that secularism is one of the basic features of the Constitution.	Reasoning
Consistent with the constitutional philosophy, subsec.	None
(3) of S. 123 the Representation of Peoples Act, 1951 treats an appeal to the electorate to vote on the basis of the religion, race, caste or community of the candidate or the use of religious symbols as a corrupt practice.	None
Even a single instance of such a nature is enough to vitiate the election of the candidate.	None
Similarly, subsection (3A) of S. 123 provides that promotion of, or attempt to promote, feelings of enimity or hatred between different classes of citizens of India on grounds of religion, race, caste, community or language by a candidate or his agent etc. for the furtherance of the prospects of the election of that candidate is equally a corrupt practice.	Reasoning
S. 29A provides for registration of associations and bodies as political parties with the Election Commission.	None
Every party contesting elections and seeking to have a uniform symbol for all its candidates has to apply for registration.	None
While making such application, the association or body has to affirm its faith and allegiance to the principles of socialism, secularism and demoracy among others.	None
Since the Election Commission appears to have made some other orders in this behalf after the conclusion of arguments and because those orders have not been placed before us or debated, we do no. wish to say anything more on this subject.	None
ARTICLE 74(2)  	None
ITS MEANING AND SCOPE The Constitution of India has introduced parliamentary democracy in this country.	None
The parliamentary democracy con notes vesting of real power of governance in the Prime Minister and council of his ministers who are very often drawn from the majority party in Parliament.	None
Some Jurists indeed refer to it decisively as Primeministerial form of Government.	None
In such a democracy, the head of the State, be he the King or the President, remains a constitutional head of the State.	None
He acts in accordance with the aid and advice tendered to him by the council of ministers with the Prime Minister at its head.	None
This is what Cl.	None
(1) of Art.	None
74 provided even before it was amended by the 42nd (Amendment) Act.	None
It was so understood and interpreted in Ramjawaya Kapoor v. State of Punjab, and in Shamsher Singh .	None
The 42nd Amendment merely made explicit what was already implicit in Cl.	None
(1).	Reasoning
The 44th Amendment inserted a proviso to Cl.	None
(1) which too was in recognition of an existing reality.	None
It empowers the President to require the council of ministers to reconsider the advice tendered by them.	None
The advice tendered on such reconsideration is made binding upon the President.	None
Since Cl.	None
(2) of Art.	None
74 has to be read understood having regard to its context, it would be appropriate to read with the clauses of Art.	None
74 as they stand now Council of Ministers to aid and advice President  (1) There shall be a Council of Ministers with the Prime Minister at the head to aid and advice the President who shall, in the exercise of his functions, act in accordance with such advice Provided that the President may require the Council of Minister to reconsider such advice, either generally or otherwise, and the President shall act in accordance with the advice tendered after such reconsideration.	None
The question whether any, and if so what, advice was tendered by Ministers to the President shall not be inquired into in any Court.	None
(Emphasis added) Article 53(l) of the Constitution says that the executive power of the Union shall be vested in the President and shall be exercised by him either directly or through officers subordinate to him in accordance with this Constitution.	None
Cl.(2), however, declares that without prejudice to Cl.(i), the supreme command of the Armed Forces of the Union shall be vested in the President and that the exercise of such power shall be regulated by law.	None
Clause (1) of Art. 77 provides that all executive action of the Government of India shall be expressed to be taken in the names of the President.	None
Cl.	None
(2) then says that all orders made and other instruments executed in the name of the President shall be authenticated in such manner as may be specified in the Rules to be made by the President.	None
It further provides that the validity of an order or instrument which is authenticated in accordance with the said Rules shall not be called in question on the ground that it is not an order or instrument made or executed by the President.	None
Rules have been made by the President as contemplated by this clause contained in Notification No.	None
SO 2297 dated November 11, 1958 (as amended from time to time).	None
Several officers of the Government have been empowered to authenticate the orders and other instruments to be made and executed in the name of the President.	None
Cl.	None
(3) requires the President to make Rules for the more convenient transaction of the business of the Government of India and for allocation among Ministers of the said business.	None
In other words, Rules have to be made by the President under Cl.	None
(3) for two purposes, viz., (1) for the more convenient transaction of the business of the Government of India and (b) for the allocation among Ministers of the said business Rules of business have indeed been made as required by this clause and the business of the Government of India allocated between several Ministers.	None
Yet another Article which requires to be noticed in this connection is Art. 361 which declares that the President shall not be answerable to any Court for the exercise and performance of the powers and duties of his office or for any act done or purporting to be done by him in the exercise and performance of those powers and duties.	None
No. criminal proceedings can be instituted or continued against the President in any Court while he is in office, nor is he subject to any process for his arrest or imprisonment.	None
Article 78 specifies the duties of the Prime Minister as regards the furnishing of information to President and certain other matters.	None
Cl.	None
(1) obliges the Prime Minister to communicate to the President all decisions of the Council of Ministers relating to the administration of the affairs of the Union and proposals for legislation.	None
Cl.	None
(b) says that Prime Minister shall furnish such information as the President may call for with respect to the matters communicated under Cl.	None
(a).	None
Cl.	None
(c) obliges the Prime Minister, if required by the President, to submit any matter for reconsideration of the Council of Ministers which has not been considered by it.	None
The President is clothed with several powers and functions by the Constitution.	None
It is not necessary to detail them to expect to say that Art.	None
356 is one of them.	None
When Art. 74(1) speaks of the President acting in the exercise of his functions, it refers to those powers and functions.	None
Besides the Constitution, several other enactments too confer and may hereinafter confer, certain powers and functions upon the President.	None
They too will be covered by Art. 74(l).	None
To wit, the President shall exercise those powers and discharge those functions only on the aid and advice of the council of ministers with the Prime Minister at its head.	None
Article 361 is the manifestation of the theory prevalent in English Law that King can do no.	None
wrong and, for that reason, beyond the process of the Court.	None
Any and every action taken by the President is really the action of his Ministers and subordinates.	None
It is they who have to answer for, defend and justify any and every action taken by them in the name of the President, if such action is questioned in a Court of Law.	None
The President cannot be called upon to answer for or justify the action.	None
It is for the council of minister to do so.	None
Who comes forward to do so is a matter for them to decide and for the Court to be satisfied about it.	None
Normally speaking, the Minister or other official or authority of the Ministry as is entrusted with the relevant business of the Government, has to do it.	None
Article 53(l)	None
insofar as says that the executive power of the Union, which vests in the President, can be exercised by him either directly or through officers subordinate to him in accordance with the Constitution stresses the very idea.	None
Even where he acts directly, the President has to act on the aid and advice of the Council of Ministers or the Minister concerned, as the case may be.	None
(Advice tendered by a Minister is deemed to be the advice tendered by the Council of Ministers in view of the principle of joint responsibility of the cabinet council of ministers).	None
If such act is questioned in a Court of Law, it is for the Minister concerned (according to Rules of Business) or an official of that Ministry to defend the Act.	None
Where the President acts through his subordinates, it is for that subordinate to defend the action.	None
Articles 74 and 77 are in a sense complimentary to each other, though they may operate in different fields.	None
Art. 74(l) deals with the acts of the President done in exercise of his functions , whereas Art. 77 speaks of the executive action of the Government of India which is taken in the name of the President of India.	None
Insofar as the executive action of the Government of India is concerned, it has to be taken by the Minister official to whom the said business is allocated by the Rules of Business made under Cl.	None
(3) of Art.	None
77 for the more convenient transaction of the business of the Government of India.	None
All orders issued and the instruments executed relatable to the executive action of the Government of India have to be authenticated in the manner and by the officer empowered in that behalf.	None
The President does not really come into the picture so far as Art.	None
77 is concerned.	None
All the business of the Government of India is transacted by the Ministers or other officials empowered in that behalf, of course, in the name of the President.	None
Orders are issued, instruments are executed and other acts done by various Ministers and officials, none of which may reach the President or may be placed before him for his Consideration.	None
There is no. occasion in such cases for any aid and advice being tendered to the President by the Council of Ministers.	None
Though expressed in the name of the President, they are the acts of the Government of India.	None
They are distinct from the acts of the President in the exercise of his functions contemplated by Art.	None
74.	None
Of course, even while acting in exercise of his functions, the President has to act in accordance with the aid and advice tendered by the Council of Ministers with the Prime Minister at its head.	None
He is thus rendered a constitutional  or a titularhead.	None
(The proviso to Cl.	None
(1) no. doubt empowers him to require the Council of Ministers to reconsider such advice, either generally or in any particular cases, but if and when the Council of Ministers tendered the advice on such reconsideration, he is bound by it.)	None
Then comes Cl.	None
(2) of Art. 74 which says that the question whether any, and if so, what advice was tendered by the Ministers to the President shall not be enquired into in any Court.	None
The idea behind Cl.	None
(2) is this the Court is not to enquire  it is not concerned with whether any advice was tendered by any Minister or Council of Ministers to the President, and if so, what was that advice.	None
That is a matter between the President and his Council of Ministers.	None
What advice was tendered, whether it was required to be reconsidered, what advice was tendered after reconsideration, if any, what was the opinion of the President, whether the advice was changed pursuant to further discussion, if any, and how the ultimate decision was arrived at, are all matters between the President and his Council of Ministers.	None
They are beyond the ken of the Court.	None
The Court is not to go into it.	None
It is enough that there is an order act of the President in appropriate form.	None
It will take it as the order act of the President.	None
It is concerned only with the validity of the order and legality of the proceedings or action taken by the president in exercise of his functions and not with what happened in the inner councils of the President and his Ministers.	None
No. one can challenge such decision or action on the ground that it is not in accordance with the advice tendered by the Ministers or that it is based on no. advice.	None
If, in a given case, the President acts without, or contrary to, the advice tendered to him, it may be a case warranting his impeachment, but so far as the Court is concerned, it is the act of the.	None
President.	None
(We do not wish to express any opinion as to what would be the position if in the unlikely event of the Council of Ministers itself questioning the action of the President as being taken without, or contrary, to their advice.)	None
Clause (2) of Art. 74, understood its proper perspective, is thus confined to a limited aspect.	None
It protects and preserves the secrecy of the deliberations between the President and his Council of Ministers.	None
In fact, Cl.	None
(2) is a reproduction of subsec.	None
(4) of S. 10 of the Government of India Act, 1935.	None
(The Government of India Act did not contain a provision corresponding to Art.	None
74(l) as it stood before or after the Amendments aforementioned).	None
The scope of Cl.	None
(2) should not be extended beyond its legitimate fields.	None
In any event, it cannot be read or understood as conferring an immunity upon the council of ministers or the Minister Ministry concerned to explain, defend and justify the orders and acts of the President done in exercise of his functions.	None
The limited provision contained in Art. 74(2) cannot override the basic provisions in the Constitution relating to judicial review.	None
If and when any action taken by the President in exercise of his functions is questioned in a Court of Law, it is for the Council of Ministers to justify the same, since the action or order of the President is presumed to have been taken in accordance with Art. 74(l).	None
As to which Minister or which official of which Ministry comes forward to defend the order action is for them to decide and for the Court to be satisfied about it.	None
Where, of course, the act order questioned is one pertaining to the executive power of the Government of India, the position is much simpler.	None
It does not represent the act order of the President done taken in exercise of his functions and hence there is no. occasion for any aid or advice by the Ministers to him.	None
It is the act order of Government of India, though expressed in the name of the President.	None
It is for the concerned Minister or Ministry, to whom the function is allocated under the Rules of Business to defend and justify such action order.	None
The orders and acts of President of India made and taken in exercise of his functions are generally expressed as having been ordered or taken by the President of India whereas the executive action of the Government of India is expressed to have been ordered or taken by the Government of India in the name of the President of India .	None
This difference in form is only indicative  and not obligatory or mandatory.	None
Section 123 of the Evidence Act, in our opinion, is in no. manner relevant in ascertaining the meaning and scope of Art. 74(2).	None
Its field and purpose is altogether different and distinct.	None
S. 123 reads thus Evidence as to affairs of StateNo one shall be permitted to give any evidence derived from unpublished official records relating to any affairs of State, except with the permission of the officer at the head of the department concerned, who shall give or withhold such permission as he thinks fit.	None
Evidence Act is a preConstitution enactment.	None
S. 123 enacts a rule of English Common Law that no.	None
one shall be permitted to give evidence derived from unpublished official records relating to affairs of State except with the permission of the concerned head of the department.	None
It does not prevent the head of department permitting it or the head of the department himself giving evidence on that basis.	None
The law relating to S. 123 has been elaborately discussed in several decisions of this Court and is not in issue herein.	None
Our only object has been to emphasise that Art. 74(2) and S. 123 cover different and distinct areas.	None
It may happen that while justifying the Governments action in Court, the Minister of the concerned official may Claim a privilege under Section 123.	None
If and when such privilege is claimed, it will be decided on its own merits in accordance with the provisions of that Section.	None
But, Art. 74(2) does not and cannot mean that the Government of India need not justify the action taken by the President in the exercise of his functions because of the provision contained therein.	None
No. such immunity was intended  or is provided  by the clause.	None
If the act or order of the President is questioned in a Court of Law, it is for the Council of Ministers to justify it by disclosing the material which formed the basis of the act order.	None
The Court will not ask whether such material formed part of the advice tendered to the President or whether that material was placed before the President.	None
The Court will not also ask what advice was tendered to the President, what deliberations or discussions took place between the President and his Ministers and how was the ultimate decision arrived at.	None
The Court will only see what was the material on the basis of which the requisite satisfaction is formed and whether it is relevant to the action under Art. 356(l).	None
The Court will not go into the correctness of the material or its adequacy.	None
Even if the Court were to come to a different conclusion on the said material, it would not interfere since the Article speaks of satisfaction of the President and not of the Court.	None
In our respectful opinion, the above obligation cannot be evaded by seeking refuge under Art. 74(2).	None
The argument that the advice tendered to the President comprises material as well and, therefore, calling upon the Union of India to disclose the material would amount to compelling the disclosure of the advice is, if we can say so respectfully, to indulge in sophistry.	None
The material placed before the President by the Minister Council of Ministers does not thereby become part of advice.	None
Advice is what is based upon the said material.	None
Material is not advice.	None
The material may be placed before the President to acquaint him  and if need be to satisfy him that the advice being tendered to him is the proper one.	None
But it cannot mean that such material, by dint of being placed before the President in support of the advice, becomes advice itself.	None
One can understand if the advice is tendered in writing in such a case that writing is the advice and is covered by the protection provided by Art. 74(2).	None
But it is difficult to appreciate how does the supporting material becomes part of advice.	None
The respondents cannot say that whatever the President sees or whatever is placed before the President becomes prohibited material and cannot be seen or summoned by the Court.	None
Art. 74(2) must be interpreted and understood in the context of entire constitutional system.	None
Undue emphasis and expansion of its parameters would engulf valuable constitutional guarantees.	None
For these reasons, we find if difficult to agree with the reasoning in State of Rajasthan on this score, insofar as it runs contrary to our holding.	None
ARTICLE 356 AND JUDICIAL REVIEW Judicial review of administrative and statutory action is perhaps the most important development in the field of public law in the second half of this century.	None
In India, the principles governing this jurisdiction are exclusively Judgemade.	None
A good amount of debate took place before us with respect to the applicability, scope and reach of judicial review visavis the proclamation issued by the President under Art.	None
356 of the Constitution.	None
A large volume of caselaw and legal literature has been placed before us.	None
Though it may not be possible to refer to all that material, we shall refer to relevant among them at the appropriate place.	None
One of the contentions raised by the Union of India in Writ Petition No. 237 of 1993 (filed by Sri Sunderlal Patwa and others in Madhya Pradesh High Court questioning the proclamation) and other writ petitions is that inasmuch as the action under Article 356 is taken on the subjective satisfaction of the President and further because the President cannot be sued in a Court of Law by virtue of Article 361, the impugned proclamation is not justiciable.	None
This argument is, however, not pressed before us.	None
It is also averred that since the Parliament has approved the said proclamation, the Court ought not to entertain the writ petition and or examine the correctness or otherwise of the Presidential proclamation.	None
(This contention has been further elaborated and pressed before us, as we shall mention hereinafter).	None
Article 74(2) is relied upon to submit that the material on which the President based the requisite satisfaction cannot be compelled to be produced in Court.	None
(This contention has already been dealt with by us.)	None
It is also submitted that the report of the Governor which forms the basis of action under Article 356 and the material upon which it is based cannot be called in question by virtue of Article 361  (urged in a modified form).	None
Sri K. Parasaran, learned counsel appearing for the Union of India conceded that the action of the President under Article 356 cannot be said to be beyond judicial review and judicial scrutiny.	Arguments of Respondent
He, however, submitted that having regard to the nature of the function, the high constitutional status of the authority in whom the power is vested and the exigencies in which the said action is taken, the Court ought not to go into the question of the advisability of the action or into the adequacy of the material on which it is based.	Arguments of Respondent
The Presidential action, counsel submitted, is not susceptible to normal rules of judicial review, having regard to the political nature of the action and absence of any judicially manageable standards.	Arguments of Respondent
There may be several imponderables in the situation, which the Court cannot weigh.	Arguments of Respondent
The Presidents action under Article 356 cannot be equated to administrative action of a Government official.	Arguments of Respondent
It is exercise of a constitutional function by the highest dignitory of the nation, the President of India.	Arguments of Respondent
May be, the learned counsel submitted, in a case like Meghalaya (transferred Cases Nos. 5 and 7 of 1992), the Court may interfere where the invalidity of action is demonstrable with reference to the orders of this Court, i.e., where the invalidity is writ large on its face.	Arguments of Respondent
But, generally speaking, the Court is illfitted to Judge the material on which the action is based to determine whether the said material warranted the action taken.	Arguments of Respondent
The Court cannot sit in judgment over the prognosis of the President for that matter, of the Union Council of Ministers that the situation in a given State was one in which the Government of that State could not be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	Arguments of Respondent
This is an instance, the learned counsel continued, where the Constitution has committed a particular power to the President to be exercised in his discretion in certain specified situations a power flowing from the obligation cast by Article 355 upon the Union of India to ensure that the Government of every State is carried on in accordance with the provisions of this Constitution.	Arguments of Respondent
The President is oathbound to protect and preserve the Constitution.	Arguments of Respondent
Placed as he is and having regard to the material which is available to him alone  and also because he alone is best fitted to determine on the basis of material before him whether the situation contemplated by Article 356(1) has arisen  the matter must be left to his judgment and good sense, He alone is presumed to possess the estate politicalcumadministrative expertise necessary for a proper and sound exercise of the said power.	Arguments of Respondent
Judicial approach, which the Courts are trained to adopt, is not suited to the function under Article 356.	Arguments of Respondent
The Courts would be better advised to leave the function to those to whom it is entrusted by the Constitution.	Arguments of Respondent
The President of India has to be trusted.	Arguments of Respondent
Of course, President in Article 356(1) means the Union Council of Ministers by virtue of Article 74(l) but that makes little difference in principle.	Arguments of Respondent
That is the system of Government we have adopted.	Arguments of Respondent
There is no. reason to believe that the highest authority like the President of India  i.e., the Union Council of Ministers would not act fairly and honestly or that they would not act in accordance with the spirit and scheme of the Constitution.	Arguments of Respondent
Sri Parasaran further submitted that where a particular proclamation is questioned, the burden of establishing its invalidity lies upon the petitioner.	Arguments of Respondent
It is for him to produce the material to substantiate his contentions.	Arguments of Respondent
By virtue of Article 74(2), the Court would not enquire into the advice tendered by the Ministers to the President leading to the issuance of the impugned proclamation.	Arguments of Respondent
The advice comprises and is based upon certain material and information.	Arguments of Respondent
The advice and material cannot be separated.	Arguments of Respondent
If the Court cannot enquire into the advice, it cannot also call upon the Union of India to disclose that material.	Arguments of Respondent
The learned counsel submitted further that there is a distinction between judicial review of administrative action and judicial review of constitutional action.	Arguments of Respondent
The decisions of this Court relating to judicial review of administrative or statutory action and discretion cannot be applied to judicial review of constitutional action.	Arguments of Respondent
Appeal against such action, properly and truly speaking, must, and should always be, to the ultimate political sovereign  the people.	Arguments of Respondent
261.	Arguments of Respondent
Sri P. P. Rao, learned counsel for the State of Madhya Pradesh while adopting the contentions of Sri K. Parasaran concentrated mainly upon the secular nature of our Constitution, with the sequiter that nonsecular policies, programmes and acts of political parties place such parties outside the pale of constitutionalism.	Arguments of Respondent
He submitted that.	Arguments of Respondent
by adopting such policies and programmes and by indulging in nonsecular course of action, the governments run by such parties render themselves amenable to action under Article 356.	Arguments of Respondent
According to the learned counsel, B.J.P.s election manifestos, together with the speeches and acts of their leaders and cadres make it a nonsecular party and, therefore, the dismissal of their Government in Madhya Pradesh is perfectly justified.	Arguments of Respondent
Sri Andhyarujina, learned AdvocateGeneral of Maharashtra submitted that the doctrine of political question has not been givenup altogether by the decision of the U. S. Supreme Court in Baker v. Carr, )1962) 7 Law Ed 2nd 663).	Arguments of Respondent
All that the decision has done is to limit the area of operation of the said doctrine.	Arguments of Respondent
The dismissal of a State Government or dissolution of a State Legislative Assembly is essentially a political question, the validity and correctness whereof cannot be adjudged with reference to any known judicial standards and or dicta.	Arguments of Respondent
Such matters be best left to the wisdom of the President and ultimately of the people.	Arguments of Respondent
It is for the people to judge whether a particular dismissal or dissolution was just or not.	Arguments of Respondent
