356 or that it was mala fide or that there was no. material on which the President could be reasonably satisfied that the dismissal of these State government was indeed called for, submitted the learned counsel for Union of India and other respondents.	Arguments of Respondent
363.	Arguments of Respondent
With a view to demonstrate his submission that judicial approach and judicial processes are not appropriate to judge the Various situations calling for action under Art. 356, Sri Parasaran gave the following scenario the Union Council of Ministers was apprehensive of the safety of the disputed structure once the B.J.P. came to power in Uttar Pradesh.	Arguments of Respondent
It was repeatedly reminding the State government in that behalf.	Arguments of Respondent
All the time, the State government and its Chief Minister were assuring the Union of India, the National Integration Council and even the Supreme Court, through statements, affidavits and representations that the State government was committed to the safety of the disputed structure and that it would ensure that no. harm comes to it.	Arguments of Respondent
The Central Government was sceptical of these assurances.	Arguments of Respondent
But suppose it had taken action under Art. 356, dismissed the Government of Uttar Pradesh some time prior to December 6, 1992 on the ground that it did not have any faith in those assurances, the Court could well have found fault with the action.	Arguments of Respondent
The Court would have said that there was no. basis for their apprehension when the State government itself represented by the Chief Minister and other high officials was repeatedly assuring everyone including the Supreme Court that they will protect the structure.	Arguments of Respondent
There was no. reason not to believe them and that the action taken under Art.	Arguments of Respondent
356 is, therefore, unjustified, being based upon mere suspicion.	Arguments of Respondent
But, in the event, the central government did not take action and the disputed structure was demolished with enormous consequences and repercussions.	Arguments of Respondent
This only shows, says Sri Parasaran, that these matters cannot be weighed in golden scales and that Judicial approach and assumptions are illsuited to such situations.	Arguments of Respondent
364.	Arguments of Respondent
Having given our earnest consideration to the matter, we are of the opinion that the situation which arose in these three States consequent upon the demolition of the disputed structure is one which cannot be assessed properly by the court.	None
Sri Parasaran is right in his submission that what happened on 6th December, 1992 was no.	None
ordinary event, that it was the outcome of a sustained campaign carried out over a number of years throughout the country and that it was the result of the Speeches, acts and deeds of several leaders of B.J.P. and other Organisations.	None
The event had serious repurcussions not only within the country but outside as well.	None
It put in doubt the very secular credentials of this nation and its government  and those credentials had to be redeemed.	None
The situation had many dimensions, social, religious, political and international.	None
Rarely do such occasions arise in the life of a nation.	None
The situation was an extraordinary one its repercussions could not be foretold at that time.	None
Nobody could say with definiteness what would happen and where?	None
The situation was not only unpredictable, it was a fastevolving one.	None
The communal situation was tense.	None
It could explode anywhere at any time.	None
On the material placed before us, including the reports of the Governors, we cannot say that the President had no. relevant material before him on the basis of which he could form the satisfaction that the B.J.P. governments of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh cannot dissociate themselves from the action and its consequences and that these governments, controlled by one and same party, whose leading lights were actively campaigning for the demolition of the disputed structure, cannot be dissociated from the acts and deeds of the leaders of B.J.P. In the then prevailing situation, the Union of India thought it necessary to ban certain organisations including R.S.S. and here were governments which were headed by persons who swore by the values and traditions of the R.S.S. and were giving overt and covert support to the associate communal organisation (vide report of the Governor of Madhya Pradesh).	None
The Governor of Himachal Pradesh reported that the Chief .Minister himself is a member of R.S.S The Governor of Rajasthan reported that the ban on R.S.S. and other organisations was not being implemented because of the intimate connection between the members of the government and those organisations.	None
The three Governors also spoke of the part played by the members of the government in sending and welcoming back the kar sevaks.	None
They also expressed the opinion that these governments cannot be expected, in the circumstances, to function objectively and impartially in dealing with the emerging law and order situation, which had all the ominous makings of a communal conflagration.	None
If the President was satisfied that the faith of these B.J.P. governments in the concept of secularism was suspect in view of the acts and conduct of the party controlling these governments and that in the volatile situation that developed pursuant to the demolition, the government of these States cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution, we are not able to say that there was no. relevant material upon which he could be so satisfied.	None
The several facts stated in the counter affidavits and the material placed before us by the Union of India cannot be said to be irrelevant or extraneous to the purpose for which the power under Art.	None
356 is to be exercised.	None
As pointed out by us supra (under the heading Judicial Review), we cannot question the correctness of the material produced and that even if part of it is not relevant to the action, we cannot interfere so long as there is some relevant material to sustain the action.	None
If the President was satisfied that the governments, which have already acted contrary to one of the basic features of the Constitution, viz., secularism, cannot be trusted to do so in future, it is not possible to say that in the situation then obtaining, he was not justified in believing so.	None
This is precisely the type of situation, which the court cannot judge for lack of judicially manageable standards.	None
The court would be well advised to leave such complex issues to the President and the Union Council of Ministers to deal with.	None
It was a situation full of many imponderables, nuances, implications and intricacies.	None
There were too many ifs and buts which are not susceptible of judicial scrutiny.	None
It is not correct to depict the said proclamations as the outcome of political vendetta by the political party in power at the centre against the other political party in power in some States.	None
Probably in such matters, the ultimate arbiter is the people.	None
The appeal should be to the people and to People alone.	None
The challenge to the proclamation relating to these three States is, therefore, liable to fail.	None
The fact that the ban was held to be unsustainable later on by the appropriate Tribunal is not relevant while judging the situation obtaining in the day following the demolition.	None
We may summarise our conclusions now Article 356 of the Constitution confers a power upon the President to be exercised only where he is satisfied that a situation has arisen where the government of a State cannot be carried on in accordance with the provisions of the Constitution.	Reasoning
Under our Constitution, the power is really that of the Union Council of Ministers with the Prime Minister at its head.	None
The satisfaction contemplated by the Article is subjective in nature.	None
The power conferred by Art.	Arguments of Petitioner
356 upon the President is a conditioned power.	None
It is not an absolute power.	None
The existence of material which may comprise of or include the report(s) of the Governor  is a precondition.	None
The satisfaction must be formed on relevant material.	None
The recommendations of the Sarkaria Commission with respect to the exercise of power under Art.	None
356 do merit serious consideration at the hands of all concerned.	None
Though the power of dissolving of the Legislative Assembly can be said to be implicit in clause (1) of Art.	None
356, it must be held, having regard to the overall constitutional scheme that the President shall exercise it only after the proclamation is approved by both Houses of Parliament under clause (3) and not before.	None
Until such approval, the President can only suspend the Legislative Assembly by suspending the provisions of Constitution relating to the Legislative Assembly under subclause (c) of clause (1).	Reasoning
The dissolution of Legislative Assembly is not a matter of course.	None
It should be resorted to only where it is found necessary for achieving the purposes of the proclamation.	None
The proclamation under cl.	None
(1) can be issued only where the situation contemplated by the clause arises.	None
In such a situation, the government has to go.	None
There is no. room for holding that the President can take over some of the functions and powers of the State government while keeping the State government in office.	None
There cannot be two governments in one sphere.	None
(5)(a) Clause (3) of Article 356 is conceived as a control on the power of the President and also as a safeguard against abuse.	None
In case both Houses of Parliament disapprove or do not approve the proclamation, the proclamation lapses at the end of the twomonths period.	None
In such a case, government which was dismissed revives.	None
The Legislative Assembly, which may have been kept in suspended animation gets reactivated.	None
Since the proclamation lapses and is not retrospectively invalidated  the acts done, orders made and laws passed during the period of two months do not become illegal or void.	None
They are, however, subject to review, repeal or modification by the government Legislative Assembly or other competent authority.	None
However, if the proclamation is approved by both the Houses within two months, the government (which was dismissed) does not revive on the expiry of period of proclamation or on its revocation.	None
Similarly, if the Legislative Assembly has been dissolved after the approval under clause (3), the Legislative Assembly does not revive on the expiry of the period of proclamation or on its revocation.	None
Article 74(2) merely bars an enquiry into the question whether any, and if so, what advice was tendered by the ministers to the President.	None
It does not bar the court from calling upon the Union Council of Ministers (Union of India) to disclose to the court the material upon which the President had formed the requisite satisfaction.	None
The material on the basis of which advice was tendered does not become part of the advice.	None
Even if the material is looked into by or shown to the President, it does not partake the character of advice.	None
Article 74(2) and S. 123 of the Evidence Act cover different fields.	None
It may happen that while defending the proclamation, the minister or the concerned official may claim the privilege under S. 123.	None
If and when such privilege is claimed, it will be decided on its own merits in accordance with the provisions of S. 123.	None
The proclamation under Article 356(1) is not immune from judicial review.	None
The Supreme Court or the High Court can strike down the proclamation if it is found to be mala fide or based on wholly irrelevant or extraneous grounds.	None
The deletion of clause (5) (which was introduced by 38th (Amendment) Act) by the 44th (Amendment) Act, removes the cloud on the reviewability of the action.	None
When called upon, the Union of India has to produce the material on the basis of which action was taken.	None
It cannot refuse to do so, if it seeks to defend the action.	None
The court will not go into the correctness of the material or its adequacy.	None
Its enquiry is limited to see whether the material was relevant to the action.	None
Even if part of the material is irrelevant, the court cannot interfere so long as there is some material which is relevant to the action taken.	None
If the court strikes down the proclamation, it has the power to restore the dismissed government to office and revive and reactivate the Legislative Assembly wherever it may have been dissolved or kept under suspension.	None
In such a case, the court has the power to declare that acts done, orders passed and laws made during the period the proclamation was in force shall remain unaffected and be treated as valid.	None
Such declaration, however, shall not preclude the government Legislative Assembly or other competent authority to review, repeal or modify such acts, orders and laws.	None
The Constitution of India has created a federation but with a bias in favour of the centre.	None
Within the sphere allotted to the States, they are supreme.	None
Secularism is one of the basic features of the Constitution.	Reasoning
While freedom of religion is guaranteed to all persons in India, from the point of view of the State, the religion, faith or belief of a person is immaterial.	None
To the state, all are equal and are entitled to be treated equally.	None
In matters of State, religion has no. place.	None
No. political party can simultaneously be a religious party.	None
Politics and religion cannot be mixed.	None
Any State government which pursues unsecular policies or unsecular course of action acts contrary to the Constitutional mandate and renders itself amenable to action under Art.	None
356.	Arguments of Respondent
The proclamation dated April 21, 1989 in respect of Karnataka (Civil Appeal No. 3645 of 1989) and the proclamation dated October 11, 1991 in respect of Meghalaya (Transferred Cases Nos. 5 and 7 of 1992) are unconstitutional.	Decision
But for the fact that fresh elections have since taken place in both the States  and new Legislative Assemblies and governments have come into existence  we would have formally struck down the proclamations and directed the revival and restoration of the respective governments and Legislative Assemblies.	Decision
The Civil Appeal No. 3645 of 1989 and Transferred Cases Nos. 5 and 7 of 1992 are allowed accordingly.	Decision
Civil Appeals Nos. 193 and 194 of 1989 relating to Nagaland are disposed of in terms of the opinion expressed by us on the meaning and purport of Article 74(2) of the Constitution.	Decision
The proclamations dated January 15, 1993 in respect of Madhya Pradesh, Rajasthan and Himachal Pradesh concerned in Civil Appeals Nos. 1692, 1692A1692C of 1993, 46274630 of 1990, Transferred Case (C) No. 9 of 1993 and Transferred Case No. 8 of 1993 respectively are not unconstitutional.	None
The Civil Appeals are allowed and the judgment of the High Court of Madhya Pradesh in M.P.(C) No. 237 of 1993 is set aside.	Decision
The Transferred Cases are dismissed.	Decision
In the light of the reasons given and conclusions recorded hereinabove, we find ourselves in agreement with the conclusions 1, 2 and 4 to 7 in the judgment of our learned brother Sawant, J. delivered on behalf of himself and Kuldip Singh, J. We are also in broad agreement with conclusion No. 8 in the said judgment.	Decision
No. orders on Interlocutory Applications.	None
There shall be no. order as to costs in these matters.	Decision
